Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
No 'Iddah for a Dhimmi Woman Divorced by a Dhimmi: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Critical refinement of 'iddah ruling for dhimmi, with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a dhimmi woman must observe 'iddah after divorce by a dhimmi, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the condition of belief.

Core Idea
A dhimmi woman's 'iddah depends on her community's belief — Abu Hanifa respects their belief; the Sahibayn require it absolutely.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya refines the ruling on 'iddah for a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi. The base ruling states no 'iddah is required, but the issue is complicated by intra-Hanafi disagreements and conditions. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi law balances respect for non-Muslim beliefs with the Islamic purpose of 'iddah — protecting lineage. We will trace the arguments of Zufar, Abu Hanifa, and the Sahibayn, and see how pregnancy and community belief affect the outcome.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the starting point: 'And no 'iddah upon a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi.' This is the general principle before any conditions or qualifications.
The commentary first affirms this base ruling, then immediately introduces disagreements.
2
Analyze Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that no 'iddah is required at all. Understand his reasoning: the first 'iddah was dropped by the second marriage (if she remarried during the first 'iddah), and what has fallen cannot return. The response from the commentator refutes this by emphasizing that the first 'iddah remains obligatory and must be completed; the second marriage only temporarily suspended it.
Zufar's position is extreme; the majority (Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn) reject it.
3
Examine the main dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
After dismissing Zufar's view, the core dispute appears. Abu Hanifa holds that 'iddah is not obligatory unless the dhimmi community believes in its obligation. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that 'iddah is obligatory unconditionally. Note the proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on the principle of leaving them to their beliefs ('they are commanded to leave the people of dhimmah with what they practice'), while the Sahibayn argue that 'iddah is for the protection of Muslims who may marry her.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Identify the consensus exception: pregnancy
The passage adds a critical restriction: if the dhimmi woman is pregnant, 'iddah by delivery is obligatory by consensus. This applies to all dhimmi and even harbi women, because the lineage of the child must be protected.
This exception overrides all disagreements.
5
Apply the analogy to mahram marriage
The commentary compares this dispute to the ruling on marriage with mahrams by non-Muslims. Abu Hanifa validates such marriages if the non-Muslims believe it permissible; the Sahibayn invalidate them absolutely. This illustrates the consistent principle: Abu Hanifa respects their beliefs as long as they do not harm Muslims.
Understanding this analogy helps predict the Hanafi approach in other cross-community issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا عدة على ذميَّةٍ طلقها ذمي (١) وعند زفر لا عدة عليها أصلاً ؛ لأن العدةَ الأُولَى سقطت بالتزوج، ولم يجب بالنكاح الثاني لدليل محمد الله. ( ولا عدة على ذمية طلقها ذمي) (۱) [1]قوله : وعند زفر ... الخ ؛ قد وافق زفر له محمدا لله في وجوب نصف المهر عليه فحسب، وفي عدم وجوب العدّة للطلاقِ الثاني، لدليل ذكر في توجيه قول محمد ، وخالفه في وجوب إتمام العدة مستنداً بأن العدة الأولى قد سقط وجوبها عليها بالتزوّج الثاني، والساقط لا يعود فلا يجب عليها شيء من التربص، بل يجوز لها أن تنكح كما طلق زوجها الأول. ورد بأن وجوب العدّة الأولى بالطلاق الأوّل، فيجب إكماله، إلا أنه لم يظهر حال التزوج الثاني، فإذا طلقها ثانياً قبل الدخول صار النكاح الثاني معدوماً، فيجب عليها إكمال ما وجب أولاً مع أن في عدم إيجاب العدّة عليها مطلقاً إبطال لما هو المقصود من شرعيّة العدة، وهو عدم اختلاط الأنساب واشتباهها. كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. [٢] قوله : ولا عدة ؛ أي لا تجبُّ العدّة على ذميّة طُلقت من ذمي، فيحل تزوجها كما طلقت، مسلماً كان المتزوج أو ذمّياً. كذا في «الفتح»)، وغيره. وأورد عليه : بأنه كيف يحلّ تزوّج مسلم بها على الفور، وهو يعتقد وجوب العدة. لكافر. وأجيب عنه : بأنه إنّما يعتقد وجوبها لنفسه ولسائر المسلمين، ولا يعتقد وجوبها للكافر. هذا عند أبي حنيفة له إذا لم يكن معتقد أهلِ الدَّمة ذلك، وإن كان معتقدهم ذلك تجب عنده، وعندهما تجب مطلقاً ، ثم أصل المسألة مقيدة بعدم الحمل، فإنّ الذميّة التي طلقت من ذمي إن كانت حاملاً تجب عليها العدةُ بوضع الحمل اتفاقاً ؛ لأن في بطنها ولداً ثابت النسب، وكذا الحكم في الحربية وغيرها ممن لا عدة عليها ، فإنّه مقيد بعدم كونها ذات حمل كذا في «الهداية» وغيرها. [١]قوله : وإن كان ؛ حاصله : إنّ عدم وجوب العدّة على الذمية التي طلقها ذمي مقيد بما إذا كانوا يعتقدون ذلك : أي عدم وجوب العدّة ، وأما إن كان في اعتقادهم وجوب العدّة بالطلاق تجب عليها، والوجه فيه أنّ أهل الإسلام أمروا بأن يتركوا أهل الذمة وما يدينون، فيعامل بهم حسب ما اعتقدوا. [٢] قوله : تجب مطلقاً ؛ أي سواء كانوا اعتقدوا ذلك أو لم يعتقدوا، وهذا الخلاف نظير الخلاف في نكاح المحارم، فإنّ نكاح الكفار بالمحارم صحيح عنده إذا كانوا يعتقدون حلَّ ذلك ، حتى لا يتعرّض بهم فيه، ولا يصح عندهما.
And no 'iddah upon a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi. (1) And according to Zufar, no 'iddah upon her at all, because the first 'iddah fell due to marriage, and it is not obligatory by the second marriage according to the proof of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). (And no 'iddah upon a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi) (1) [1] He said: And according to Zufar... etc.; Zufar agreed with Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) regarding the obligation of half the dower only, and regarding the non-obligation of 'iddah for the second divorce, due to a proof mentioned in the justification of Muhammad's statement, but he disagreed regarding the completion of 'iddah, relying on the fact that the first 'iddah's obligation had fallen off due to the second marriage, and that which falls does not return, so no waiting period is obligatory upon her, rather it is permissible for her to marry as soon as her first husband divorces her. And it was refuted by saying that the obligation of the first 'iddah was by the first divorce, so it must be completed, except that it did not appear during the second marriage; so when he divorces her a second time before consummation, the second marriage becomes non-existent, so she must complete what was first obligatory, all the while that not obligating 'iddah upon her absolutely invalidates the intended purpose of legislating 'iddah, which is to prevent mixing and confusion of lineages. Thus in al-Fath and others. [2] He said: And no 'iddah; meaning 'iddah is not obligatory upon a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi, so it is permissible to marry her as soon as she is divorced, whether the one marrying is Muslim or dhimmi. Thus in al-Fath and others. And an objection was raised: how is it permissible for a Muslim to marry her immediately, while he believes in the obligation of 'iddah for the disbeliever? And it was answered: He only believes in its obligation for himself and for the rest of the Muslims, and he does not believe in its obligation for the disbeliever. This is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) if the people of dhimmah do not hold that belief; if they do hold that belief, then it is obligatory according to him, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is obligatory absolutely. Then the basis of the issue is restricted to the absence of pregnancy, for if the dhimmi woman who is divorced by a dhimmi is pregnant, then 'iddah becomes obligatory upon her by delivery by agreement, because in her womb there is a child of established lineage. And similarly the ruling applies to a harbiyya and others who do not have 'iddah - it is restricted to her not being pregnant. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. [1] He said: 'And if' - the summary of it is that the non-obligation of 'iddah upon the dhimmi woman who is divorced by a dhimmi is restricted to when they believe that: i.e., non-obligation of 'iddah. But if in their belief 'iddah is obligatory by divorce, it becomes obligatory upon her, and the reason is that the people of Islam are commanded to leave the people of dhimmah with what they practice, so they are dealt with according to what they believe. [2] He said: 'It is obligatory absolutely', i.e. whether they believe that or not, and this difference is similar to the difference regarding marriage with mahrams, for the marriage of disbelievers with mahrams is valid according to him (Abu Hanifa) if they believe in its permissibility, so that they are not interfered with therein, and it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a critical refinement of the commentary on al-Wiqaya, addressing the ruling of 'iddah for a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi. The author begins by stating the base ruling: no 'iddah is required. He then introduces the disagreement of Zufar, who holds no 'iddah at all because the first 'iddah was dropped by the second marriage, and what drops does not return. The correct position (Imam Muhammad and the majority) refutes this by emphasizing that the first 'iddah was obligatory and must be completed; the second marriage only temporarily suspended it, and if the second divorce occurs before consummation, the original 'iddah resumes. The ruling is then explained: 'iddah is not obligatory, but this is conditional. If the dhimmi community believes in 'iddah, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa it becomes obligatory. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is always obligatory. The text also clarifies that a Muslim may marry such a woman immediately, because the Muslim believes 'iddah is only for Muslims. The entire issue is further restricted to cases where she is not pregnant; if pregnant, 'iddah by delivery is required by consensus. The commentary then elaborates on the conditional nature: if the dhimmi community believes in 'iddah, she must observe it, and this is analogous to the validity of marriage with mahrams among non-Muslims: if they believe it permissible, it is valid for them according to Abu Hanifa. The lesson underscores the Hanafi distinction between obligations based on belief and the overarching purpose of 'iddah to protect lineages.

Key Terms
ذمية (dhimmiyya): A free non-Muslim woman living under the protection (dhimmah) of the Islamic state.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying, to confirm pregnancy.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim male subject of an Islamic state with a covenant of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does a dhimmi woman divorced by her dhimmi husband have to observe 'iddah?
A) Whenever she is pregnant
B) If the dhimmi community believes in the obligation of 'iddah
C) Always, irrespective of belief
D) Never, because she is a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a dhimmi woman to remarry immediately after divorce unless her community believes in 'iddah or she is pregnant, preserving the principle of leaving them to their beliefs while protecting lineage.

No 'Iddah for a Harbiyya Who Emigrates in Anger
Commentary on the ruling of 'iddah for a warring woman who emigrates to Muslim lands as a Muslim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'iddah for a harbiyya woman who becomes Muslim and emigrates, and identify the condition (mughadabah) that triggers the absence of 'iddah.

Core Idea
A harbiyya who flees in anger to dar al-Islam needs no 'iddah unless pregnant.

This lesson examines a specific ruling of the Hanafi school: a woman from enemy territory (harbiyya) who leaves her husband out of anger and enters the Islamic state as a Muslim does not have to observe an 'iddah. This is because her marriage is considered invalid upon her departure. However, if she is pregnant, she must wait until delivery to clarify lineage. Understanding this rule requires careful attention to the condition of mughadabah – the intent never to return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage and determine what ruling is being stated about the harbiyya woman. The key phrase is 'no 'iddah upon a harbiyya who comes to dar al-Islam as a Muslim'.
The ruling is that 'iddah is not required, contrary to what might be assumed.
2
Locate the condition for the ruling
The commentary clarifies that coming as a Muslim is not the real condition; the actual condition is that she leaves in anger (mughadabah) with no intention to return. Identify this condition in the source.
Muqhadabah is leaving in opposition and permanent severance.
3
Note the exception for pregnancy
The ruling is restricted to non-pregnant women. If she is pregnant, 'iddah is required until delivery. Find this exception in the text.
This is to protect lineage, a right of the child.
4
Understand the rationale (right of Muslim)
The commentary mentions that a Muslim believes in 'iddah, and that 'iddah protects a right of the human being. However, the Harbiyya's marriage is invalid, so that right does not apply here. Trace this reasoning.
This shows that the ruling is based on the invalidity of the marriage, not on the Muslim's belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا حربية خرجت إلينا مسلمة (١) عليها العدة، فلا يحل له نكاح مسلم أو كافر بها قبل انقضائها ؛ لأنّ المسلم يعتقد وجوب العدة، ووجوب العدّة فيه حق الآدمي، فإنّها وجبت صيانة لماء محترم. فالزوجة وإن كانت كافرة لا يمكن لها أن تبطل حق المسلم ، وكذا الحكم في الحربية وغيرها ممن لا عدة عليها فإنه مقيد بعدم كونها ذات حمل كذا في «الهداية» وغيرها. [٣] قوله : ولا حربية ؛ أي لا عدة على حربية خرجت إلى دار الإسلام مسلمة، فوقعت الفرقة بينهما وبين زوجها الحربي، والخروج مسلمة ليس بقيد احترازي، بل الشرط هو الخروج على سبيل المراغمة ؛ أي المغاضبة، وعلى نية أن لا تعود إلى دار الحرب أبداً. فلو خرج أحد الزوجين إلينا مسلماً أو ذمّياً ثمّ أسلم، أو مستأمناً ثم صار ذمّياً ، والآخر في دار الحرب على حربه، فقد زالت الزوجية ولم تجب العدّة على الزوجة في كلتا الصورتين. كذا في «البناية» (r)
He said: And no [iddah for a] harbiyya who comes to us as a Muslim. (1) Upon her is the 'iddah, so it is not permissible for a Muslim or a disbeliever to marry her before its expiry, because the Muslim believes in the obligation of 'iddah, and the obligation of 'iddah involves a right of the human being, for it was mandated to protect a respected fluid. So the wife, even if she is a disbeliever, cannot nullify the right of a Muslim. And similarly the ruling applies to a harbiyya and others who do not have 'iddah - it is restricted to her not being pregnant. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. [3] He said: And no [iddah for a] harbiyya; i.e., no 'iddah upon a harbiyya who comes to dar al-Islam as a Muslim, so separation occurs between her and her harbi husband, and her coming as a Muslim is not a restrictive condition, rather the condition is that she leaves in anger (mughadabah), i.e., in opposition, and with the intention never to return to dar al-harb. So if one of the spouses comes to us as a Muslim or dhimmi and then becomes Muslim, or as a musta'min (one seeking safe conduct) and then becomes a dhimmi, and the other is in dar al-harb persisting in war, then the marriage ceases and no 'iddah is obligatory upon the wife in both cases. Thus in al-Binayah. (r)
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of 'iddah for a harbiyya woman (from enemy territory) who enters dar al-Islam as a Muslim. The commentary first addresses a potential misunderstanding: it might be thought that such a woman must observe 'iddah because a Muslim would believe in 'iddah and has a right to it. However, the correct ruling is that she does not observe 'iddah, because the marriage is invalidated by her departure and conversion, and the Muslim's right is not applicable since the woman is not subject to the Muslim's belief. The condition that she comes as a Muslim is not essential; the real condition is that she leaves her husband in anger (mughadabah) with the intention of never returning. Similarly, if a husband becomes Muslim or dhimmi and the wife remains in dar al-harb, the marriage also ends without 'iddah. The ruling is further restricted: if the woman is pregnant, then 'iddah is required by delivery (not a waiting period per se) to establish lineage.

Key Terms
حربية (harbiyya): A woman from dar al-harb (territory at war with Muslims) who has no covenant of protection.
مراغمة (mughadabah): Leaving in anger and opposition, with the intention of severing ties permanently.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule that is at war with the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the general rule for a harbiyya woman who leaves dar al-harb and enters dar al-Islam as a Muslim?
A) She must observe 'iddah for three menstrual cycles
B) She does not observe 'iddah, but if pregnant she must wait until delivery
C) She must observe 'iddah only if her husband also converts
D) She must observe a four-month waiting period

Key Takeaway
A harbiyya woman who flees in anger to dar al-Islam needs no 'iddah, but pregnancy requires waiting for delivery.

Talaq — Divorce

Iddah for Dhimmi and Harbi Women: Internal Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on iddah for non-Muslim women with internal Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on iddah for dhimmi and harbi women, explain the reasoning based on 'haqq al-adami' and 'tabayun al-darayn', and apply the ruling to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Iddah is not always obligatory for non-Muslim women; belief and abode status shape the obligation in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores a critical internal Hanafi ikhtilaf: whether the waiting period (iddah) is required for non-Muslim women who separate from their husbands. The dispute hinges on two principles: the right of a human (haqq al-adami) to protect lineage, and the change of abode (tabayun al-darayn) as a cause of separation. By understanding this ikhtilaf, you will see how Hanafi jurists apply usul al-fiqh to real cases and how the school's flexibility accommodates different social contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two separate cases: a dhimmi woman divorced by her dhimmi husband, and a harbi woman who emigrates to Dar al-Islam as a Muslim. For each, note the ruling under Abu Hanifa and under the Sahibayn.
The cases are treated separately because different principles apply.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For the dhimmi woman: iddah is not obligatory unless her community believes in iddah. For the harbi woman: no iddah at all, even if she becomes Muslim and emigrates, unless she is pregnant. The key reason: a harbi's lineage is not 'protected' (mā' muhtaram), so the human right behind iddah does not exist.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning uses the principle 'yutraku wa ma yadeenun' (leave them with their beliefs) for dhimmis.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that iddah is obligatory in both cases, absolutely. Their reasoning: the change of abode (tabayun al-darayn) is a cause of separation that necessitates iddah, especially when the woman becomes Muslim and is now bound by Islamic law.
They also analogize this to the dispute on marriage to mahrams among non-Muslims.
4
Analyze the proofs (talil)
Abu Hanifa focuses on the purpose of iddah (preserving lineage) and defines a harbi as legally like an inanimate object. The Sahibayn emphasize the legal effect of the separation itself. Identify which usul category each proof belongs to (istiḥsān vs qiyās).
This dispute is a classic example of applying different legal maxims to the same issue.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a harbi woman becomes Muslim and emigrates, under Abu Hanifa she may remarry immediately; under the Sahibayn she must wait through an iddah. For a dhimmi woman whose community does not believe in iddah, Abu Hanifa allows immediate remarriage; the Sahibayn require iddah regardless.
This difference has direct consequences for women in these situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب العدة
ولا حربية خرجت إلينا مسلمة
هذا عند أبي حنيفة له إذا لم يكن معتقد أهلِ الدَّمة ذلك، وإن كان معتقدهم ذلك تجب عنده، وعندهما تجب مطلقاً ، ولا حربية" خرجت إلينا مسلمة عليها العدة، فلا يحل له نكاح مسلم أو كافر بها قبل انقضائها ؛ لأنّ المسلم يعتقد وجوب العدة، ووجوب العدّة فيه حق الآدمي، فإنّها وجبت صيانة لماء محترم. فالزوجة وإن كانت كافرة لا يمكن لها أن تبطل حق المسلم ، ثم أصل المسألة مقيدة بعدم الحمل، فإنّ الذميّة التي طلقت من ذمي إن كانت حاملاً تجب عليها العدةُ بوضع الحمل اتفاقاً ؛ لأن في بطنها ولداً ثابت النسب، وكذا الحكم في الحربية وغيرها ممن لا عدة عليها ، فإنّه مقيد بعدم كونها ذات حمل كذا في «الهداية» وغيرها. [١]قوله : وإن كان ؛ حاصله : إنّ عدم وجوب العدّة على الذمية التي طلقها ذمي مقيد بما إذا كانوا يعتقدون ذلك : أي عدم وجوب العدّة ، وأما إن كان في اعتقادهم وجوب العدّة بالطلاق تجب عليها، والوجه فيه أنّ أهل الإسلام أمروا بأن يتركوا أهل الذمة وما يدينون، فيعامل بهم حسب ما اعتقدوا.
[٢] قوله : تجب مطلقاً ؛ أي سواء كانوا اعتقدوا ذلك أو لم يعتقدوا، وهذا الخلاف نظير الخلاف في نكاح المحارم، فإنّ نكاح الكفار بالمحارم صحيح عنده إذا كانوا يعتقدون حلَّ ذلك ، حتى لا يتعرّض بهم فيه، ولا يصح عندهما.
[٣] قوله : ولا حربية ؛ أي لا عدة على حربية خرجت إلى دار الإسلام مسلمة، فوقعت الفرقة بينهما وبين زوجها الحربي، والخروج مسلمة ليس بقيد احترازي، بل الشرط هو الخروج على سبيل المراغمة ؛ أي المغاضبة، وعلى نية أن لا تعود إلى دار الحرب أبداً. فلو خرج أحد الزوجين إلينا مسلماً أو ذميّاً ثمّ أسلم، أو مستأمناً ثم صار ذمّياً ، والآخر في دار الحرب على حربه، فقد زالت الزوجية ولم تجب العدّة على الزوجة في كلتا الصورتين. كذا في «البناية»
(۱) أو ذميّة أو مستأمنة طلّقها أو مات عنها ؛ لأن العدة لفراش الزوج المحترم ولا احترام له ؛ ولذا كان محلاً للتمليك ، وتمامه في (الدر المنتقى» (١ : ٤٧٠).
(۲) (الهداية» (٤ : ٣٣٣ - ٣٣٤). (۳) (البناية (٤ : ٧٩٦ - ٧٩٧).
وفي المسألة خلافٌ لهما كما في المسألة السابقة، فعندهما تجب عليها العدة إن خرجت إلينا مهاجرة ؛ لأنّ تباين الدارين أحد أسباب الفرقة، فيجب عليها العدة به، لا سيما إذا صارت مسلمة ؛ لكونها مكلّفة بالأحكام الشرعيّة، بخلاف ما إذا هاجر الرجلُ وتركها في دار الحرب، فإنّه لا تجب العدّة عليها ؛ لعدم تبليغ أحكام الشرع إليها. وله : إن العدة وجبت حيث وجبت كان فيها حق بني آدم ، والحربي ملحق بالجمادِ شرعاً، فلا تتربص صيانة لمائه ، بل يجوز لها أن تتزوج كما وصلت إلينا، نعم إن كانت حاملاً وجب عليها التربّص ؛ لأنّ في بطنها ولداً ثابت النسب، كمـا «الهداية»(۱) وحواشيها.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Iddah. And no harbi woman who came out to us as Muslim [is obligated]. This is according to Abu Hanifa: if the people of dhimma do not believe that [iddah is obligatory]; and if their belief is that, then it is obligatory according to him. According to them (the two Sahibayn), it is absolutely obligatory. And a harbi woman who came out to us as Muslim is obligated to observe iddah, so it is not permissible for a Muslim or a disbeliever to marry her before its completion, because the Muslim believes in the obligation of iddah, and the obligation of iddah contains a right of a human (haqq al-adami), for it was legislated to protect respected semen. So the wife, even if she is a disbeliever, cannot nullify the right of a Muslim. Then the origin of the issue is restricted by non-pregnancy; for a dhimmi woman who is divorced by a dhimmi, if she is pregnant, she must observe iddah by delivering the child by agreement, because in her belly is a child of established lineage. Similarly the ruling applies to a harbi woman and others who have no iddah, but it is restricted by not being pregnant. Thus in al-Hidayah and others. [1] His saying: 'and if he was'; the summary: that the non-obligation of iddah for a dhimmi woman divorced by a dhimmi is restricted to when they believe that, i.e., non-obligation of iddah; but if in their belief iddah is obligatory upon divorce, it is obligatory upon her. The reasoning is that the people of Islam were commanded to leave the people of dhimma with what they believe in, so they are treated according to what they believed. [2] His saying: 'it is absolutely obligatory'; i.e., whether they believed that or did not believe. And this dispute is similar to the dispute in marriage to mahrams; for the marriage of disbelievers to mahrams is valid according to him (Abu Hanifa) if they believe in its permissibility, so that they are not interfered with, but it is not valid according to them (the two). [3] His saying: 'and no harbi woman'; i.e., no iddah for a harbi woman who came to the Abode of Islam as Muslim, thereby separation occurs between her and her harbi husband. And 'coming as Muslim' is not a restrictive condition; rather the condition is leaving out of anger (mughadaba) and with the intention never to return to the Abode of War. So if one of the spouses comes to us as Muslim or dhimmi then converts, or as a musta'min then becomes dhimmi, while the other is in the Abode of War in his state of war, the marriage is dissolved and iddah is not obligatory on the wife in both cases. Thus in al-Binayah. (1) Or if she is a dhimmi or musta'mina whom he divorced or who died, because iddah is for a respected husband's bed, and he has no respect; therefore he is subject to ownership. And the completion is in al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:470). (2) al-Hidayah (4:333-334). (3) al-Binayah (4:796-797). And in this issue there is a dispute of the two (Sahibayn) as in the previous issue; according to them, iddah is obligatory upon her if she came out to us as a muhajira (emigrant), because the difference of the two abodes (tabayun al-darayn) is one of the causes of separation, so iddah is obligatory due to it, especially if she became Muslim, because she is obligated by the legal rulings. Unlike if a man emigrates and leaves her in the Abode of War, then iddah is not obligatory upon her, because the legal rulings were not conveyed to her. And according to him (Abu Hanifa): iddah, where it is obligatory, therein is a right of humans (haqq bani adam), and the harbi is legally treated as inanimate, so she does not wait for the protection of his semen; rather it is permissible for her to marry as soon as she arrives to us. Yes, if she is pregnant, she must wait, because in her belly is a child of established lineage. As in al-Hidayah (1) and its glosses.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the obligation of iddah for non-Muslim women after separation from their husbands due to divorce or change of abode. The author first states a general principle: a dhimmi woman (a non-Muslim under Muslim protection) who is divorced by her dhimmi husband is not obligated to observe iddah according to Abu Hanifa, unless her community believes in iddah. If they do believe, then it becomes binding. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that iddah is absolutely obligatory regardless of belief. For a harbi woman (from enemy territory) who comes to the Muslim abode as a Muslim and Muslim immigrant, the Sahibayn say she must observe iddah, while Abu Hanifa says no. The key reasoning: Abu Hanifa argues that iddah is legislated to protect human lineage (preservation of respected semen), and that right belongs to the Muslim husband, not the harbi. A harbi is treated like a non-entity in Islamic law, so no iddah is needed. However, if she is pregnant, iddah by delivery is obligatory by consensus because the child's lineage is established. The condition of 'coming as Muslim' is interpreted as leaving out of enmity (mughadabah) with the intention not to return. The practical result: according to Abu Hanifa, a harbi woman who becomes Muslim and emigrates can marry immediately, while the Sahibayn require a waiting period. The passage also analogizes the dispute to the ruling on marriage to mahrams among non-Muslims. Throughout, the author draws from al-Hidayah and al-Binayah, showing the textual basis.

Key Terms
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry, to ensure no pregnancy from the previous marriage.
حربية (harbiyyah): A woman from the Abode of War (Dar al-Harb), i.e., a non-Muslim country not under Muslim treaty.
ذمية (dhimmiyyah): A non-Muslim woman living under Muslim rule with a contract of protection (dhimma).
حق الآدمي (haqq al-adami): A right belonging to a human being (as opposed to a right of Allah), here specifically the right of a husband over the lineage of his offspring.
تباين الدارين (tabayun al-darayn): The difference of abodes (Dar al-Islam vs Dar al-Harb) leading to legal consequences, including dissolution of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does a dhimmi woman who is divorced by her dhimmi husband not need to observe iddah?
A) When she is pregnant
B) When her community does not believe in the obligation of iddah
C) When her husband converts to Islam
D) When she is a harbi woman

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's internal ikhtilaf on iddah for non-Muslim women shows how differing applications of usul principles (haqq al-adami vs tabayun al-darayn) produce distinct rulings, each with strong textual support.

beginner
10 min
Ihdad: Definition, Ruling, and Evidence
Defines ihdad and cites hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ihdad, identify which women must observe it, and explain the dalil for its obligation for widows and ba'in divorcees.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for widows and ba'in divorcees—mourning through abandoning adornment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah introduces the chapter on ihdad, defining the term linguistically and legally, and then establishing its obligation via hadith. Understanding who must observe ihdad and on what proof is essential for correctly applying this ruling in marital dissolution cases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic definition
The commentator explains that ihdad derives from both triliteral (hadda) and quadriliteral (ahadda) roots, meaning to abandon adornment. Identify the key forms.
Both verb forms are used in the sources.
2
Identify the women who must observe ihdad
The text states 'mu'taddat al-ba'in wa al-mawt' – women in 'iddah due to ba'in divorce or death. Distinguish from raj'i divorcees who are not required.
Raj'i divorce remains a valid marriage; no ihdad needed.
3
Examine the evidence for the widow
The hadith from Bukhari and Muslim explicitly commands four months and ten days of mourning with prohibitions on dyed clothes, kohl, and perfume, indicating wujub.
The prohibition and command imply obligation (al-amr yufidu al-wujub).
4
Examine the evidence for the ba'in divorcee
The author of al-Hidayah uses the hadith forbidding henna for the woman in 'iddah, considering henna as perfume, to analogize to all adornment. This is cut off but the inference is clear.
Henna is considered a form of adornment; its prohibition extends to all zīnah.
5
Apply the ruling
State the outcome: ihdad is wajib for widows and ba'in divorcees; it means refraining from wearing attractive clothes, applying makeup, perfume, and other adornments.
The ruling is the same for both categories in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحداد
وتحد معتدة البائن، والموت
[١]قوله : وتحد؛ - بكسر الحاء المهملة وتشديد الدال المهملة – مضارع من الإحداد، وهذا شروع في بيان ما ينبغي للمعتدة في العدة بعد الفراغ من بيان من تجب عليه ومَن لا تجب عليه، وذكر أزمنتها.
والإحداد: عبارة عن تركِ الزينة لمعتدة ، يقال : احتدت المرأة إحداداً فهي مُحدّة ومحد إذا تركت الزينة لموته، وحدت تحدّ وتَحُدّ حداداً بالكسر فهي حاد. كذا في «المصباح» وغيره. والحاصل أنه جاء من الرباعي ومن الثلاثي من باب ضرب ، ونصر وبه عرف أن قوله : تَحدّ يمكن أن يكون من الإحداد كما ذكرنا ، وأن يكون - بفتح التاء وكسر الحاء من باب فريفر، وأن يكون - بفتح التاء وضم الحاء - من باب مَدَّ يَمُد.
[٢] قوله : معتدة البائن والموت ؛ أي المعتدّة التي طلقها زوجها أو مات عنها، أمّا إحداد المتوفى عنها زوجها، فالأصل فيه حديث: «لا يحل لامرأة تؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن تحدّ على ميت فوق ثلاث ليال إلا على زوجها أربعة أشهر وعشراً، ولا تلبس ثوباً مصبوغاً إلا ثوب عصب، ولا تكتحل ولا تمس طيباً (۳)، أخرجه الشيخان وغيرهما. وفي باب أخبار كثيرة مروية في كتب السنن.
وأما المعتدة عن بائن، والمراد بهما أعم من المطلقة بواحدة بائنة، أو ثنتين بائنتين ، ومن المطلقة بثلاث، ومن المختلعة، فاستدل لوجوب الإحدادِ عليها صاحب «الهداية» بحديث : «إنّ النبي له الله نهى المعتدة أن تختضب بالحنّاء»، وقال: «الحنّاء طيب» لإطلاقه،
Chapter on Ihdad. And she observes ihdad (mourning) for the ba'in divorcee and for death. [1] His saying: 'Tahiddu' - with kasra of the ha and tashdid of the dal - is the imperfect verb from ihdad. This is a beginning of explaining what is required for the woman in 'iddah after finishing the explanation of who must observe it and who not, and mentioning its durations. And ihdad is an expression of abandoning adornment for a woman in 'iddah. It is said: ihtadat al-mar'atu ihdadan, so she is muhiddah and muhid if she abandons adornment due to his death; and haddat tahiddu/tahuddu hidadan (with kasra), so she is hadd. Thus in al-Misbah and others. The upshot is that it comes from the quadrilateral and from the trilateral from the pattern of daraba and nasara, and by that it is known that his word 'tahiddu' can be from ihdad as we mentioned, and can be with fatha of the ta and kasra of the ha from the pattern of [unclear], and can be with fatha of the ta and dhamma of the ha from the pattern of madda yamuddu. [2] His saying: 'the woman in 'iddah of ba'in and death' means the woman whose husband divorced her or died from her. As for the ihdad of a woman whose husband died, the basis for it is the hadith: 'It is not permissible for a woman who believes in Allah and the Last Day to mourn a dead person more than three nights except for her husband - four months and ten days, and she should not wear a dyed garment except 'asb cloth, nor apply kohl, nor touch perfume.' Recorded by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others. And in this chapter many reports are narrated in the books of sunan. As for the woman in 'iddah due to ba'in - and the meaning includes the one divorced by one or two irrevocable divorces, the one divorced by three, and the one divorced by khul' - the author of al-Hidayah used as evidence for the obligation of ihdad upon her the hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade the woman in 'iddah to dye with henna,' and he said: 'Henna is perfume' due to its absolute nature.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on ihdad (mourning) in the context of 'iddah. The commentator first clarifies the linguistic derivation of the word 'tahiddu' from both the triliteral (hadda yahuddu) and quadriliteral (ahadda yuhiddu) roots, noting that both forms are used. The core definition of ihdad is the abandonment of adornment (zīnah) for the woman observing 'iddah. The passage then identifies the women who must observe ihdad: the ba'in divorcee (including those divorced by one or two irrevocable divorces, by three divorces, or by khul') and the widow. The proof for the widow's ihdad is the well-known hadith in Bukhari and Muslim that prohibits mourning beyond three days except for a husband, for whom it is four months and ten days, with specific prohibitions on wearing dyed garments, kohl, and perfume. For the ba'in divorcee, the author of al-Hidayah adduces the hadith forbidding the woman in 'iddah from dyeing with henna, reasoning that henna is a perfume. This argument is cut off at the end of the page, but the implication is that since adornment is prohibited, ihdad is wajib for her as well. Note that the raj'i divorcee is not required to observe ihdad because her marriage is still intact; she may return to her husband without a new contract. The practical outcome: ihdad is obligatory (wajib) for widows and ba'in divorcees, and it involves refraining from wearing colorful or attractive clothes, applying makeup or perfume, and similar adornments.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): Abandonment of adornment (zīnah) by a woman in 'iddah, specifically for a ba'in divorcee or widow.
حَدَّ (hadda): The triliteral verb form of ihdad, meaning to mourn or abstain from adornment.
معتدة البائن (mu'taddat al-ba'in): A woman in 'iddah who has been irrevocably divorced (ba'in talaq), including those divorced by one or two irrevocable divorces, three divorces, or khul'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The evidence for both is from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is wajib for widows and ba'in divorcees, established by sound hadith for widows and by valid analogy for ba'in divorcees.

Ihdad after Ba'in Divorce: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on who must observe ihdad and debate with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ihdad for ba'in divorce, explain its proof, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for all women in iddah, including ba'in divorce, because grief is for the blessing of marriage.

In this passage, we examine a classic dispute: must a woman divorced irrevocably (ba'in) observe mourning (ihdad)? The Hanafi school says yes; the Shafi'i says no. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the reasoning and refutes the opposing view. We will work through the arguments to understand the Hanafi position and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its scope
Determine the ruling and who it applies to: adult, Muslim, free or slave women in ba'in or death iddah. Note the exclusions from the commentary: minor, non-Muslim, insane, and women in raj'i iddah (by consensus).
The matn says 'adult' (kabira), but the commentator suggests 'accountable' (mukallafa) is more precise.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection and its evidence
The Shafi'i argue that ihdad is not wajib because the woman is hurt by separation and does not grieve. They cite a hadith from al-Nasa'i that prohibits adornment, but its authenticity is disputed.
Al-Zayla'i noted he could not find the hadith, suggesting it is weak.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal is that ihdad is to show grief at the loss of the blessing of marriage (ni'mat al-nikah), which is present in both death and divorce. The emotion of the woman is irrelevant; the legal basis is the loss of marital benefit.
This is the core difference in reasoning.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical consequence: Hanafi women must avoid all adornment (perfume, jewelry, silk, etc.) during ba'in iddah; Shafi'i women may use them.
This affects daily life for the duration of the iddah.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the school, as per al-Hidayah and later authorities.
While the dispute is legitimate, the operative fatwa for Hanafis is wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كبيرة ((۱) مسلمة حرة ) أو (لا) : فقوله : أولا : عطف على قوله : حرة، وعند الشافعي لا حدادا على معتدة البائن ، بترك الزينة. وذكر السروجي في «شرحه»: إنّه حديث رواه النسائي بلفظ : «نهى المعتدة عن الكحل والدهن والخضاب بالحناء»، وقال: «الحناء طيب»، ونسبه الزَّيْلَعِي في تخريجه إلى الوهم، وقال: إني لم أجده. وفي ذكر البائن احتراز عن الرجعي، فإنّ معتدة الرجعي لا إحداد عليها اتفاقاً ، بل لها أن تتزين ليرغب الزوج إليها فيراجعها. قوله : كبيرة ؛ الأولى أن يقول : مكلّفة ؛ لتخرج الصغيرة والكافرة والمجنونة ؛ فإنّه لا يجب الإحداد عليهن. (۲) قوله : حرّة ؛ أي سواء كانت حرّة أو أمة، فإنّ الإحداد شرع لإظهار التأسف بموت زوجها أو بالبينونة، والأمة في ذلك كالحرّة. (۳) قوله : عطف على قوله : حرّة ؛ صرّح به ؛ لئلا يتوهم أنه عطف على كبيرة ، فيختل المعنى. قوله : لا حداد ؛ لأنه وجب إظهاراً للتأسف على فوتِ الزوج، وفي بُعْدِها إلى مماته، والمبتوتة قد أوحشها زوجها بالإبانة فلا تأسف على فوته ومفارقته، ونحن نقول : إنّ الحداد لإظهار التأسف على فواتِ نعمة النكاح ، وهو موجود في الإبانة أيضاً. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : بترك الزينة ؛ متعلق بتحد، والزينة ما تزيّن به المراة من الحلي بجميع أنواعه، والحرير، وغير ذلك كالامتشاط واستعمال الطيب وغير ذلك.
Adult, Muslim, free or not (i.e., slave), by avoiding adornment. [Commentary:] So he said: 'or not' is a conjunction to 'free'. According to al-Shafi'i: There is no ihdad for a woman in ba'in iddah, by avoiding adornment. And al-Saruji mentioned in his commentary that it is a hadith narrated by al-Nasa'i with the wording: 'He forbade the woman in iddah from kohl, oil, and dyeing with henna,' and he said: 'Henna is perfume.' Al-Zayla'i attributed this to error in his takhrij and said: I did not find it. Mentioning ba'in is a precaution against raj'i, for the woman in raj'i iddah has no ihdad by consensus, rather she may adorn herself so that the husband may desire her and take her back. He said: 'Adult' – it would have been better to say 'accountable' to exclude the minor, the disbeliever, and the insane, for ihdad is not obligatory upon them. (2) He said: 'Free' – i.e., whether free or slave, for ihdad is legislated to show grief at the death of her husband or at separation, and the slave is like the free in this. (3) He said: 'Conjunction to free' – he explicitly stated this so that it is not mistakenly thought to be a conjunction to 'adult', which would spoil the meaning. He said: 'No ihdad' – because it is wajib to show grief at the loss of the husband, and in her case the husband is far from death, and the irrevocably divorced woman has been hurt by her husband through separation, so she does not grieve over his loss and separation. We say: ihdad is to show grief at the loss of the blessing of marriage, and that is present in separation as well. This is in al-Hidayah. He said: 'By avoiding adornment' – it is related to ihdad, and adornment is what a woman adorns herself with, such as jewelry of all types, silk, and other things like combing, using perfume, etc.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of ihdad (mourning) for a woman whose marriage ends by ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. The author first explains that the phrase 'or not' in the original matn is conjoined to 'free', meaning the ruling applies to both free and slave women. He then presents the Shafi'i position: there is no ihdad for a woman in ba'in iddah. As evidence, al-Saruji mentions a hadith from al-Nasa'i that prohibits kohl, oil, and henna for a woman in iddah, but al-Zayla'i considers this a mistake. The author then clarifies that the mention of 'ba'in' is to exclude raj'i divorce, where there is no ihdad by consensus. He critiques the matn's phrasing 'adult' (kabira), suggesting 'accountable' (mukallafa) would be better to exclude minors, disbelievers, and insane women. He confirms that slaves are included in the ruling. The core of the dispute: the Shafi'i argue that a woman divorced irrevocably has been hurt by her husband, so she does not grieve his loss; the Hanafi response is that ihdad expresses grief at the loss of the blessing of marriage, which remains. Finally, he defines 'adornment' (zina) broadly to include jewelry, silk, combing, perfume, etc.

Key Terms
إحداد (ihdad): Abstention from adornment during iddah as a sign of mourning.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.
زينة (zina): Adornment including jewelry, silk, perfume, and other beautification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A woman in iddah after a raj'i divorce
B) A woman in iddah after a ba'in divorce

D) A non-Muslim woman

Key Takeaway
Ihdad is wajib on a woman in ba'in iddah because grief is for the blessing of marriage, not the person.

Prohibited Adornments in Ihdad and Necessity Exceptions
Listing prohibited adornments in ihdad and exceptions for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six prohibited adornments during ihdad and apply the exception for necessity using the Hanafi principle of darura.

Core Idea
During ihdad, avoid all adornments, but necessity (darura) permits otherwise prohibited items for genuine needs.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh commentary specifies what a woman in ihdad (mourning period) must avoid. It lists specific adornments like saffron-dyed clothes, henna, perfume, oil, and kohl. Crucially, it then introduces an exception: genuine necessity (e.g., eye treatment, lack of clothing) makes the prohibited permissible. This reflects the Hanafi legal method of balancing strict rules with human exigency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The woman in ihdad must avoid all adornments (zina). The text enumerates specific items that fall under this prohibition.
2
List the prohibited items
The text mentions: clothes dyed with saffron (muzaf far) or safflower (mu asfar), henna, all types of perfume, hair oil, and kohl. The commentary adds that any color with a pleasant scent is included.
Non-adorned colors like black and blue are explicitly allowed.
3
Recognize the general exception
The phrase 'except due to an excuse' (illa bi-udhr) applies to all items. The principle is: necessities permit prohibitions (al-darurat tubih al-mahdhurat).
4
Analyze the examples of necessity
Specific examples are given: eye pain allows kohl; body itch allows wearing silk; headache allows oil and combing; no other garment allows wearing colored clothes to cover awrah.
The exception is limited to the extent of the need.
5
Apply the Hanafi maxim
The overarching legal maxim is that necessity overrides prohibition. This is a core principle in Hanafi fiqh.
Necessity is assessed on a case-by-case basis.

No Ihdad for Umm Walad in Iddah
Ruling that umm walad does not observe ihdad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ihdad for an umm walad in iddah and explain why it is not wajib.

Core Idea
Ihdad is not required for umm walad because there was no marriage to mourn.

Iddah is an obligatory waiting period for certain women, but not all iddahs require ihdad (mourning). This lesson clarifies that an umm walad (a female slave who bore her master's child) does not observe ihdad when her master dies or emancipates her, even though she must observe iddah. The reason lies in the purpose of ihdad: to express grief over the loss of marriage. Since the umm walad was never married to her master, there is nothing to grieve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ihdad (mourning) during iddah for an umm walad. The ruling is that it is not wajib.
The text explicitly states 'الإحداد غير واجب'.
2
Identify the reason (ta'lil)
Ihdad is legislated to show grief over the loss of the blessing of marriage. The umm walad had no marriage with her master, so the basis is absent.
The text says: 'لأنّ شرعيته لإظهار التأسف على فوت نعمة النكاح، ولا نكاح هاهنا'.
3
Scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whether the umm walad becomes free by her master's act or by his death. Both are included under 'عتق'.
The commentator clarifies that the author's phrase 'معتدة العتق' covers both scenarios.

The conclusion is that the umm walad, upon the death of her master or his emancipation, even though iddah is obligatory upon her, ihdad is not obligatory, because its legislation is to show grief at the loss of the blessing of marriage, and there is no marriage here.

Commentary
This passage clarifies that ihdad is not required for a woman who is in iddah due to emancipation (whether by the master's act or his death). The author corrects a potential misunderstanding that the ruling applies only when the master actively emancipates; it includes both emancipation and death of the master. The reason (ta'lil) is that ihdad is prescribed to express grief over the loss of the blessing of marriage, but the umm walad had no marriage with her master, so there is no basis for ihdad. This is a definitive ruling in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
معتدة العتق (mu'taddat al-'itq): A woman observing iddah due to becoming free (by emancipation or master's death).

Prohibitions During Iddah and Exceptions
Rulings on prohibitions during iddah and who is exempt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibitions during iddah according to the Hanafi school, identify the exceptions for necessity and for certain categories of women, and explain the underlying rationale of 'ta'assuf'.

Core Idea
Ihdad is wajib for valid marriages, but exempt for umm walad and fasid nikah.

A woman in iddah must abandon adornment as a sign of mourning (ihdad). This passage from al-Wiqaya and its commentary detail the specific prohibitions—dyed clothes, henna, perfume, oil, kohl—and crucially, the exceptions: genuine necessity (medical need) and two categories where ihdad is not required at all. Understanding these nuances prevents imposing undue hardship while preserving the legal purpose of mourning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its rationale
The passage states that a woman in iddah from a valid marriage must abstain from adornment. The rationale (ta'assuf) is to express grief over the loss of marital benefits.
This rationale drives the later exemptions.
2
List the specific prohibitions
The text enumerates: wearing saffron-dyed or safflower-dyed clothes, applying henna, using perfume, applying oil to hair, and applying kohl to eyes.
The commentary clarifies that any fragrant colour is included; plain black/blue clothes are allowed.
3
Apply the exception of necessity (darurah)
The phrase 'illa bi-'udhr' qualifies all prohibitions. Medical needs (eye pain, itching, headache) make the prohibited act permissible. If only a dyed garment is available, it may be worn to cover awrah.
Necessity overrides the prohibition temporarily.
4
Identify the categorical exemptions from ihdad
Two types of iddah do not require ihdad: (1) iddah of umm walad (due to death or freeing of master), and (2) iddah from a fasid (invalid) marriage.
In both cases, there is no valid marriage to mourn.
5
Understand the legal reasoning for exemptions
Ihdad is legislated to show grief over loss of marriage. For umm walad, the iddah is for freedom, not loss of marriage. For fasid marriage, the marriage is void and must be annulled, so no grief is required.
This aqli (rational) proof ties the ruling to its cause.

Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, detailing the prohibitions incumbent upon a woman during her iddah (waiting period) and the exceptions to these prohibitions. The main ruling: a woman in iddah must avoid all forms of adornment (zina), specifically wearing clothes dyed with saffron or safflower, applying henna, using perfume and oils, and applying kohl to the eyes. These are prohibited because they constitute beautification, which contradicts the state of mourning for the lost marriage. However, the exception ('illa bi-'udhr) applies to all these prohibitions: if there is a genuine necessity (darura), such as an eye ailment requiring kohl, an itchy body requiring silk (temporarily), or a headache needing oil and combing, then the prohibited act becomes permissible. Even wearing a safflower-dyed garment is allowed if it is the only available cover for awrah. The commentary then addresses two exceptions where ihdad (mourning) is not required at all: (1) when a woman is observing iddah due to the death or emancipation of her master (umm walad) — her iddah is for freedom, not for the loss of marriage, so no grief is expressed; (2) when the iddah is due to an invalid (fasid) marriage — such a marriage is legally void and must be annulled, so there is no marriage to grieve over. The term 'tasaffuf' (grief) is explained as sadness over the loss of marriage benefits, which is absent in these cases. The analysis follows the author's sequence, starting with the general prohibition, then the necessity exception, then the two categorical exemptions.

Key Terms
الإحداد (al-ihdad): The state of a woman abstaining from adornment and beautification during her iddah period as a sign of mourning for the loss of marriage.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; upon her master's death or emancipation, she observes iddah (of emancipation) but is exempt from ihdad because her iddah is not due to loss of marriage.
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage (fasid) that must be annulled; the woman in iddah from such a marriage does not have to perform ihdad because there is no legitimate marriage to grieve over.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because an invalid marriage is legally void and must be annulled, so there is no marriage to grieve over.
C) Because she is still considered married.
D) Because she is a slave and ihdad is only for free women.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires ihdad for women in iddah from valid marriages, but exempts those with no marriage to mourn (umm walad, fasid marriage).

Proposing to a Woman in Iddah: Direct vs Indirect
Ruling on khitbah during iddah with Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on proposing to a woman in iddah, distinguish between direct proposal (haram) and indirect hints (ta'rid, permissible with conditions), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding revocable divorce.

Core Idea
Direct proposal to a woman in iddah is haram; indirect hints are allowed except for a woman in revocable divorce.

This lesson examines the ruling on proposing marriage (khitbah) to a woman during her iddah period. The primary source is the Quranic verse (2:235), which explicitly permits indirect hints (ta'rid) while prohibiting direct proposals. However, the Hanafi school further refines this rule: a woman in revocable divorce (raj'i) is still legally married, so even ta'rid is impermissible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct legal practice.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
Identify the ruling on proposing to a woman in iddah: direct proposal is haram; indirect hints (ta'rid) are permissible. This is the apparent meaning of the matn.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
The verse (Al-Baqarah 2:235) is the primary dalil. It explicitly states 'no sin upon you in what you hint of proposal to women' (ta'rid) and prohibits direct proposals and secret promises.
The exact wording is quoted in the source.
3
Analyze the verse's implications
The verse permits ta'rid, prohibits direct khitbah, and prohibits secret agreements. Understand how the verse is used to derive the ruling.
This is wajh al-dalalah.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The matn's apparent generality (all women in iddah) is restricted by later Hanafi authorities (al-Bahr, al-Nahr, etc.): ta'rid is not allowed for a woman in revocable divorce (raj'i) because she remains married.
This is a tarjih based on caution.
5
Apply the practical consequence
Determine when ta'rid is permissible: only for women in irrevocable iddah (ba'yn talaq or death). For revocable iddah, even ta'rid is haram.
The thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تُخْطَبُ معتدة إلا تعريضاً
[1] قوله : ونكاح ؛ أي لا إحداد على معتدة نكاح فاسد بالطلاق أو الفرقة ؛ لأنَّ مثل هذا النكاح يجب رفعه شرعاً، فلا يتأسف على فواته. [٢] قوله : فلا تأسف ؛ مصدر على وزن التفعّل ، بمعنى الحزن والحسرة، أو
مضارع مؤنث محذوف أحد التائين من التأسف ، والضمير إلى المعتدة. [۳] قوله : ولا تُخطب ؛ بصيغة المجهول من الخطبة - بالكسر - ، وهو طلب المرأة للنكاح، والأصل فيه قوله : ( وَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِيمَا عَرَّضْتُم بِهِ مِنْ خِطْبَةِ النِّسَاءِ أَوْ أَكْنَنتُمْ فِي أَنفُسِكُمْ عَلِيمَ اللَّهُ أَنَّكُمْ سَتَذْكُرُونَهُنَّ وَلَكِن لَّا تُوَاعِدُوهُنَّ سِرًّا إِلَّا أَن تَقُولُوا قولا مَعْرُوفًا وَلَا تَعْزِمُوا عُقدَةَ النِّكَاحِ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ الْكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ ) . دلّت الآية على حرمة النكاح في العدة، وعلى حرمة المواعدة سراً، وهي أن يأخذ عليها عهداً وميثاقاً أن تحبس نفسها له، ولا تنكح غيره، وعلى حرمة الخطبة صراحة، وعلى جواز التعريض كقوله : إنّي فيك لراغب، وإنك على لكريمة، وإن الله سائق إليك رزقاً، ونحو ذلك، من غير أن يشافهها أو يخاطبها بالتزويج. كذا نقل عن ابن عباس ومجاهد عند ابن المنذر وابن جرير وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهم، والآثار في هذا الباب مبسوطة في «الدر المنثور للسيوطي (٤ ) قوله : إلا تعريضاً؛ ظاهره أن جوازه يعمّ كلّ معتدّة، لكن ذكر في «البحر» (4) و«النهر» و«المعراج» و«الفتح») وغيرها : إنّ التعريض لا يجوز في مطلقة الرجعي ؛
And a woman in iddah should not be proposed to except by indirect hints (ta'rid). [1] Explanation: 'and (invalid) marriage' – meaning there is no ihdad for a woman observing iddah from an invalid marriage due to divorce or separation, because such a marriage must be annulled by the Shari'ah, so one does not grieve over its loss. [2] Explanation: 'so no grief' – verbal noun on the pattern tafa''ul, meaning sadness and regret, or feminine present tense with one of the two taa's dropped from ta'assuf, and the pronoun refers to the woman observing iddah. [3] Explanation: 'and let her not be proposed to' – in the passive voice from khitbah (with kasr), which is asking for a woman in marriage. The basis for this is His saying: {And there is no sin upon you if you hint at [a proposal to] women or keep it within yourselves. Allah knows that you will mention them. But do not make a secret promise to them except that you say a word that is appropriate. And do not resolve on the tie of marriage until the prescribed period has reached its end.} The verse indicates the prohibition of marriage during the iddah, and the prohibition of secret promise (which is taking a covenant and pledge from her to reserve herself for him and not marry another), and the prohibition of direct khitbah, and the permissibility of indirect hinting, such as saying: 'I have a desire for you,' or 'You are a noble woman,' or 'Allah is bringing sustenance to you,' and the like, without directly addressing her or speaking of marriage. This is transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas and Mujahid as quoted by Ibn al-Mundhir, Ibn Jarir, Ibn Abi Shayba, and others. The narrations on this topic are detailed in al-Durr al-Manthur by al-Suyuti. Explanation: 'except by indirect hint' – apparently it is permissible for every woman in iddah, but it is mentioned in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, al-Mi'raj, al-Fath, and others that indirect hinting is not permissible for a woman in a revocable divorce.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, addressing the ruling on proposing marriage (khitbah) to a woman who is in her iddah period. The main ruling: it is haram to directly propose marriage to a woman in iddah, but it is permissible to make indirect hints (ta'rid) that suggest one's interest without explicit mention of marriage. The proof is the Quranic verse (Al-Baqarah: 235), which explicitly permits ta'rid and prohibits direct proposals or secret promises. The verse is analyzed: it forbids (1) marriage during iddah, (2) secret agreements that bind the woman, and (3) direct proposal; it allows indirect hints. The commentary cites the exegesis of Ibn Abbas and Mujahid. However, a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted: while the apparent meaning of the matn suggests that all women in iddah may receive ta'rid, later authorities (such as those in al-Bahr, al-Nahr, al-Mi'raj, and al-Fath) state that ta'rid is not permissible for a woman in a revocable divorce (raj'i talaq) because she is still legally married until her iddah ends. Therefore, for a woman in revocable divorce, even ta'rid is not allowed, as she remains under her husband's authority. The passage ends abruptly with this point, indicating that further discussion is cut off. The commentary thus clarifies that the exception for ta'rid applies to women whose iddah is from a final separation (irrevocable divorce or death), not to those in revocable iddah.

Key Terms
التعريض (al-ta'rid): Indirect hinting or alluding to something without explicit statement; in the context of khitbah, it refers to words that imply a desire for marriage without directly saying 'I want to marry you'.
مطلقة رجعي (mutallaqa raj'iyya): A woman divorced with a revocable divorce (raj'i), meaning the husband can take her back during the iddah without a new contract; she is still considered a wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because she is not in iddah.
B) Because she is still considered married until the iddah ends, and hinting at marriage would interfere with the marriage bond.
C) Because she has already been divorced irrevocably.
D) Because the Quran prohibits all forms of proposal during iddah.

Key Takeaway
Direct proposal to a woman in iddah is haram; indirect hints are permissible except for a woman in revocable divorce, who remains legally married until iddah ends.

Rulings on 'Iddah: Void Marriage and Proposing Indirectly
Sharh of Al-Wiqayah explaining two rulings on 'iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mourning for 'iddah from a void marriage and explain the Hanafi position on direct vs. indirect proposals, including the intra-madhhab dispute on its scope.

Core Idea
No mourning for void marriage's 'iddah; proposals must be indirect—but not for revocable divorcees.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on two distinct rulings. The first clarifies that a woman in 'iddah from a void marriage does not observe mourning (ihdad) because the marriage was invalid and its dissolution is not a loss. The second ruling, grounded in Quran 2:235, distinguishes between direct proposals (haram) and indirect expressions (permissible). However, later Hanafi authorities introduce an important restriction: indirect proposal is not allowed for a woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce, as it may lead to confirming the divorce. We will explore the reasoning and the school's nuanced application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings from the matn
The passage states: (1) No ihdad for 'iddah from a void marriage; (2) A woman in 'iddah is only to be proposed to indirectly.
2
Understand the basis for no ihdad
The marriage is fasid and must be nullified; hence there is no cause for grief or mourning. This is a logical deduction from the nature of void marriage.
3
Examine the primary proof for the proposal ruling
Quran 2:235 explicitly permits indirect suggestion (ta'rid) and forbids explicit proposal and secret contracts. The verse's general wording covers all women in 'iddah.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi controversy on ta'rid's scope
Some later authorities (al-Bahr, al-Nahr, al-Fath) restrict ta'rid to widows, excluding revocable divorcees due to the possibility of raj'ah. Others (al-Ikhtiyar) extend it to irrevocably divorced women.
5
Apply the tarjih and identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that ta'rid is impermissible for revocable divorcees. You must know which position governs fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونكاح فاسد، ولا تُخْطَبُ معتدة إلا تعريضاً. ونكاح فاسد؛ لأنه واجب الرفع فلا تأسف على فوته. ولا تُخْطَب معتدة إلا تعريضاً. قوله: ونكاح؛ أي لا إحداد على معتدة نكاح فاسد بالطلاق أو الفرقة؛ لأن مثل هذا النكاح يجب رفعه شرعاً، فلا يتأسف على فواته. قوله: فلا تأسف؛ مصدر على وزن التفعّل، بمعنى الحزن والحسرة، أو مضارع مؤنث محذوف أحد التائين من التأسف، والضمير إلى المعتدة. قوله: ولا تُخطب؛ بصيغة المجهول من الخطبة - بالكسر -، وهو طلب المرأة للنكاح، والأصل فيه قوله: (وَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِيمَا عَرَّضْتُم بِهِ مِنْ خِطْبَةِ النِّسَاءِ أَوْ أَكْنَنتُمْ فِي أَنفُسِكُمْ عَلِيمَ اللَّهِ أَنَّكُمْ سَتَذْكُرُونَهُنَّ وَلَكِن لَّا تُوَاعِدُوهُنَّ سِرًّا إِلَّا أَن تَقُولُوا قولا مَعْرُوفًا وَلَا تَعْزِمُوا عُقدَةَ النِّكَاحِ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ الْكِتَبُ أَجَلَهُ). دلت الآية على حرمة النكاح في العدة، وعلى حرمة المواعدة سراً، وهي أن يأخذ عليها عهداً وميثاقاً أن تحبس نفسها له، ولا تنكح غيره، وعلى حرمة الخطبة صراحة، وعلى جواز التعريض كقوله: إني فيك لراغب، وإنك علي لكريمة، وإن الله سائق إليك رزقاً، ونحو ذلك، من غير أن يشافهها أو يخاطبها بالتزويج. كذا نقل عن ابن عباس ومجاهد عند ابن المنذر وابن جرير وابن أبي شيبة وغيرهم، والآثار في هذا الباب مبسوطة في «الدر المنثور» للسيوطي. قوله: إلا تعريضاً؛ ظاهره أن جوازه يعم كل معتدة، لكن ذكر في «البحر» و«النهر» و«المعراج» و«الفتح» وغيرها: إن التعريض لا يجوز في مطلقة الرجعي؛ لأنه يفضي إلى عدة المطلق، فعلى هذا يختص جواز التعريض بمعتدة الوفاة، لكن ذكر في «الاختيار» جوازه في المبتوتة أيضا.
And [there is] a void marriage, and a woman in 'iddah is not proposed to except with indirect expression. A void marriage: because it is obligatory to raise it, so do not regret its loss. And a woman in 'iddah is not proposed to except with indirect expression. His statement: 'And a marriage' means no mourning (ihdad) for a woman in 'iddah from a void marriage due to divorce or separation, because such a marriage must be nullified legally, so she should not grieve over its loss. His statement: 'do not regret' is a verbal noun on the pattern of tafa'ul, meaning sadness and regret, or a feminine imperfect verb with one of the two ta's of 'ta'asuf' omitted, and the pronoun refers to the woman in 'iddah. His statement: 'and she is not proposed to' is in the passive voice from al-khitbah (with kasrah), which is seeking a woman for marriage. Its basis is His statement: 'And there is no blame upon you for that which you indirectly suggest to women of a proposal or hold within yourselves. Allah knows that you will mention them. But do not make a secret contract with them except to say a saying that is proper. And do not resolve the knot of marriage until the prescribed term has been reached.' The verse indicates the unlawfulness of marriage during 'iddah, and the unlawfulness of secret appointment, which is taking a covenant and pledge from her that she will confine herself for him and not marry another, and the unlawfulness of explicit proposal, and the permissibility of indirect expression like saying: 'I am desirous of you, you are dear to me, and Allah will bring provision to you,' and the like, without speaking to her directly or addressing her with marriage. Thus it is transmitted from Ibn 'Abbas and Mujahid according to Ibn al-Mundhir, Ibn Jarir, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others, and the reports on this topic are detailed in 'al-Durr al-Manthur' by al-Suyuti. His statement: 'except with indirect expression' outwardly indicates that its permissibility includes every woman in 'iddah, but it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr', 'al-Nahr', 'al-Mi'raj', 'al-Fath' and others that indirect expression is not permitted for a woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce, because it leads to confirming the divorce. Thus on this basis, the permissibility of indirect expression is specific to the widow in 'iddah, but it is mentioned in 'al-Ikhtiyar' that it is also permissible for the irrevocably divorced woman.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining two rulings from the matn. The first ruling states that a woman who is in 'iddah from a void marriage (nikah fasid)—such as a marriage that is invalid due to a defect—does not observe mourning (ihdad) because such a marriage is legally required to be nullified. Therefore, she should not regret its dissolution, and there is no waiting period of mourning as in a valid marriage. The second ruling concerns proposing to a woman during her 'iddah. Direct proposal is prohibited based on the Quranic verse (2:235). However, indirect expressions (ta'rid) are permitted, such as saying 'I am desirous of you' or 'You are dear to me,' without explicitly asking for marriage. The commentary notes that the ruling on ta'rid generally applies to all women in 'iddah, but later Hanafi scholars mention exceptions: for a woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce (raj'i), ta'rid is not allowed because it may lead to confirming the divorce. Thus, the permissibility of ta'rid is confined to widows (mu'taddat al-mawt). However, al-Ikhtiyar allows it also for an irrevocably divorced woman (mubattatah). This shows the nuance within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage that is legally invalid due to a deficiency in its conditions or pillars, but may be considered valid in some respects until annulled.
إحداد (ihdad): The mourning observance by a widow during her 'iddah, which includes refraining from beautification.
تعريض (ta'rid): An indirect expression that hints at marriage without explicit proposal, such as 'I am desirous of you'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is absolutely permissible.
B) It is not allowed because it may lead to confirming the divorce.
C) It is recommended.
D) It is reserved only for the husband.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits indirect marriage proposals during 'iddah from the Quranic verse, but restricts it for revocable divorcees to prevent harm.

Confinement Rules for Women in 'Iddah: Divorce vs. Death
States the rules of confinement for women in different types of 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on confinement for women in 'iddah of revocable divorce, irrevocable divorce, and death, and explain the Quranic proof and the hadith-based exception for widows.

Core Idea
Divorced women must stay home during 'iddah; widows may go out for necessity but must return at night.

In this passage, the commentator explains the rules of confinement for women in different types of 'iddah. The matn states that a woman in 'iddah from a revocable or irrevocable divorce cannot leave her home, while a widow may go out during the day and night but must spend the night in her home. The commentator provides the Quranic verse from Surah al-Talaq as proof for the divorced woman's confinement and cites a hadith from the Battle of Uhud to justify the widow's allowance due to her need to earn a livelihood. The passage also connects this to the permissibility of indirect marriage proposals (ta'rid), which is generally restricted to widows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of 'iddah
Read the matn and commentary to distinguish between 'iddah of revocable divorce (raj'i), irrevocable divorce (ba'in), and death (mawt). Note the confinement rule for each: divorced women must not leave their homes at all; widows may go out but must return at night.
The key difference is the widow's permission to leave, which is the focus of the explanation.
2
Locate the primary proof for divorced women
Find the Quranic verse (65:1) cited for the prohibition. Note that the verse commands 'Do not expel them from their houses, nor should they leave.' This applies to divorced women, both revocable and irrevocable.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah in its application to all types of divorce.
3
Analyze the exception for widows
Identify the hadith from the Battle of Uhud that permits widows to visit each other during the day but requires them to return at night. Understand the rationale: widows do not receive maintenance from the deceased's estate, so they need to go out for their livelihood.
This exception is based on necessity, not a direct Quranic text.
4
Connect confinement to the ruling on ta'rid
The commentator explains that because divorced women cannot leave their homes, they cannot be approached with indirect proposals (ta'rid); hence ta'rid is only permissible for widows. Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: al-Ikhtiyar allows ta'rid also for the irrevocably divorced.
'Ta'rid' is defined as an indirect expression where both literal and intended meanings are intended.
5
Understand the definition of 'bayt' (home)
The passage clarifies that 'her home' refers to the dwelling she was residing in at the start of 'iddah, regardless of ownership. This controls where she must remain.
This definition is broad and includes rented or borrowed homes.

ولا تُخْرَجُ مُعتدةُ الرّجعي والبائن من بيتها أصلاً، وتَخْرُجُ معتدةُ الموتِ في الملوين، وتبيتُ في منزلها. ولا تُخْرَجُ مُعتدةُ الرجعي والبائن من بيتها أصلاً؛ لقوله تعالى: لا تخرجوهن من بيوتهن ولا يخرجن الآية. وتخرج معتدة الموت في الملوين، وتبيت في منزلها إذ لا نفقة لها لقيام نكاح الأول، ولا في مطلقة البائن، فإنه لا يجوز لها الخروج من منزلها أصلاً، فلا يتمكن من التعريض، ولإفضائه إلى عدة المطلق، فعلى هذا يختص جواز التعريض بمعتدة الوفاة، لكن ذكر في «الاختيار» جوازه في المبتوتة أيضا. والتعريض: هو أن يقصد من لفظ معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي أو الكناية، ومن السياق معناه معرضاً به، فالموضوع له والمعرض به كلاهما مقصودان، لكن لم يستعمل اللفظ في المعرض به كقول السائل: جئت لأسلم عليك، فيقصد من السلام، ومن السياق: طلب شيء. كذا في «جامع الرموز». قوله: بيتها؛ أي السكنى التي يضاف إليها حال وجوب العدة، سواء كانت ملكاً لها أو له، أو كانت بإعارة أو إجارة. قوله: لقوله: قال في سورة الطلاق: يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ إِذَا طَلَقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَطَلِقُوهُنَّ لِعِدَّتِهِنَّ وَأَحْصُوا الْعِدَّةَ وَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ رَبَّكُمْ لَا تُخْرِجُوهُنَّ مِنْ بُيُوتِهِنَّ وَلَا يَخْرُجْنَ إِلَّا أَن يَأْتِينَ بِفَاحِشَةٍ مُبَيِّنَةٍ. قوله: في الملوين؛ بفتحات الميم واللام والواو، تثنية ملا، والمراد بهما الليل والنهار، والأصل فيه حديث: إن رجالا استشهدوا بأحد فقال نساؤهم: يا رسول الله، إنا نستوحش في بيوتنا أفنبيت عند إحدانا، فأذن لهن أن يتحدثن عند إحداهن، فإذا كان وقت النوم تأوي كل امرأة إلى بيتها، أخرجه عبد الرزاق والشافعي. قوله: إذ لا نفقة؛ حاصله: إنما أبيح لها الخروج لعدم وجوب نفقة العدة على ورثة الزوج، فتحتاج إلى الخروج لتدبير معيشتها، فأبيح لها ذلك، ولا ضرورة في
And a woman in 'iddah from a revocable or irrevocable divorce is not taken out of her home at all, and a woman in 'iddah from death goes out during the two times (day and night) and spends the night in her home. Thus, on this basis, the permissibility of indirect expression is specific to the widow in 'iddah, but it is mentioned in 'al-Ikhtiyar' that it is permissible for the irrevocably divorced as well. Do not expel them from their houses, nor should they leave unless they commit a clear immorality.' His statement: 'during the two times' with fathah on the letters, dual of 'mala', meaning the night and day. Its basis is the hadith: 'Some men were martyred at Uhud, and their wives said: O Messenger of Allah, we are feeling lonely in our homes; may we spend the night at one of our homes?' He permitted them to talk with one another at one of their homes, and when it was time to sleep, each woman would return to her home.' Recorded by 'Abd al-Razzaq and al-Shafi'i. His statement: 'because she does not have maintenance' summary: she is permitted to go out because there is no obligation of 'iddah maintenance on the heirs of the husband, so she needs to go out to manage her livelihood; thus it is permitted for her, and there is no necessity in

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the confinement rules for women in 'iddah. The matn states three categories: (1) A woman in 'iddah from a revocable divorce (raj'i) or irrevocable divorce (ba'in) must not be taken out of her home—meaning she cannot leave nor be expelled. (2) A widow in 'iddah (mu'taddat al-mawt) is allowed to go out during the day and night (fil-milwayn) but must return at night. The commentary provides the Quranic proof for the first rule from Surah al-Talaq (65:1). For the widow, the justification is that she does not receive maintenance (nafaqah) from the deceased's estate during her 'iddah, so she needs to leave for her livelihood. The hadith from the Battle of Uhud supports the allowance for widows to visit each other during the day but return at night. The commentary also ties this to the earlier discussion on ta'rid: since the revocable and irrevocably divorced women cannot leave their homes, they cannot be approached with indirect proposals; therefore, ta'rid is only possible for widows (or according to al-Ikhtiyar, also for the irrevocably divorced). Finally, the passage defines ta'rid as using a word to convey an indirect meaning, illustrated by the example 'I came to greet you' intending to request something. This definition is from Jami' al-Rumuz.

Key Terms
الملوين (al-milwayn): The dual of 'mala', meaning the night and the day.
بيتها (baytuha): The dwelling in which the woman is residing at the start of 'iddah, regardless of ownership (it could be owned by her, her husband, or rented).
التعريض (al-ta'rid): Using an expression to convey an indirect meaning, such that both the literal and intended meanings are intended, but the word is not used for the indirect one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a widow in 'iddah allowed to go out during the day and night?
A) Because she is not obliged to observe 'iddah.
B) Because she does not receive maintenance from the heirs, so she needs to manage her livelihood.
C) Because she is permitted to remarry immediately.
D) Because the Quran explicitly allows it for widows.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between divorce and death in 'iddah confinement: divorced women must stay home (Quran), while widows may go out for necessity (hadith).
