Residence During 'Iddah: Prohibitions and Exceptions
Explains residence rules during 'iddah with proofs and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on residence during 'iddah for each type of divorce, identify the exceptions, and define ta'rid.

Core Idea
A woman in 'iddah must remain in her home; widows may leave by day, and ta'rid is permissible under conditions.

The 'iddah is a period of waiting that imposes specific restrictions, the most important being residence. This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on where a woman must stay, the exceptions for widows, and the concept of indirect proposal (ta'rid).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of women in 'iddah
Recognize the categories: revocable divorce, irrevocable divorce, and widow. Note the different rulings for each.
The passage explicitly distinguishes mu'taddat al-raj'i, al-ba'in, and al-mawt.
2
Analyze the primary dalil
Examine Quran 65:1 and its imperative form to understand why staying home is wajib. Note the exception for 'fahishah' and how Hanafis interpret it narrowly.
The Hanafi principle is that the imperative indicates obligation unless overridden.
3
Understand the exceptions for widows
A widow may go out during the day and night (al-maluwayn) but must return at night. Reason: no maintenance from the deceased husband's estate.
The passage emphasizes that the widow's need to earn a livelihood permits her to leave.
4
Define ta'rid and its permissibility
Ta'rid is indirect proposal using ambiguous words. Initially restricted to widows, but al-Ikhtiyar allows it for irrevocably divorced women as well. Understand the distinction from explicit proposal (sarih).
The passage provides a detailed definition with an example: 'I came to greet you' implying a request.
5
Apply the general exceptions and sutrah rule
A woman in 'iddah may leave her home if expelled, fears loss of property, house collapse, or cannot pay rent. For irrevocable divorce, a screen (sutrah) is required when the ex-husband visits.
The sutrah prevents seclusion since the woman is now a non-mahram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تُخْرَجُ مُعتدةُ الرّجعي والبائن من بيتها أصلاً ، وتَخْرُجُ معتدةُ الموتِ في الملوين، وتبيتُ في منزلها إذ لا نفقة لها لقيام نكاح الأول، ولا في مطلقة البائن، فإنّه لا يجوز لها الخروج من منزلها أصلاً، فلا يتمكن من التعريض، ولإفضائه إلى عدة المطلق، فعلى هذا يختص جواز التعريض بمعتدة الوفاة، لكن ذكر في «الاختيار جوازه في المبتوتة أيضا.
والتعريض : هو أن يقصد من لفظ معناه الحقيقي أو المجازي أو الكناية، ومن السياق معناه معرّضاً به، فالموضوع له والمعرّض به كلاهما مقصودان، لكن لم يستعمل اللفظ في المعرّض به كقول السائل: جئت لأسلّم عليك ، فيقصد من السلام، ومن السياق : طلب شيء.
وتعتد في منزلها وقت الفرقة، والموت، والطلاق إلا أن تُخْرَجَ ، أو خافَتْ تَلَفَ مالها أو الانهدام ، أو لم تَجِدْ كراءَ البيت، ولا بُدَّ من سترة بينهما في البائن.
A woman in 'iddah from a revocable or irrevocable divorce is not to be taken out of her home at all. A widow in 'iddah goes out during the day and night and spends the night in her home, because she has no maintenance, the first marriage having ended. As for the irrevocably divorced woman, it is not permissible for her to leave her home at all, so she cannot engage in indirect proposal, leading to confusion with the divorced woman's 'iddah. Thus, the permissibility of indirect proposal is specific to the widow, but it is mentioned in al-Ikhtiyar that it is permissible also for the irrevocably divorced woman. Indirect proposal is when one intends from a word its literal or figurative meaning or allusion, and from the context a meaning alluded to, so both the literal and alluded meanings are intended, but the word is not used for the alluded meaning, like when a questioner says 'I came to greet you' intending from the greeting and from the context a request for something. And she performs 'iddah in her home at the time of separation, death, or divorce, unless she is expelled, or fears loss of her property or collapse, or does not find rent for the house. And a screen is necessary between them in the case of irrevocable divorce.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage begins with the core ruling on the residence of a woman during 'iddah. The author states that a woman undergoing 'iddah after a revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in) divorce must not be removed from her home at all. This is based on the Quranic verse in Surah al-Talaq: 'Do not expel them from their homes, nor shall they leave.' The exception is for a widow: she may go out during the day and night (the maluwayn, meaning day and night) but must return to her home at night. The rationale is that the widow receives no maintenance from the husband's estate, so she needs to go out to manage her livelihood. In contrast, the divorced woman receives maintenance during her 'iddah, so she has no need to leave. The passage then discusses indirect proposal (ta'rid) for marriage during 'iddah. The author initially restricts it to the widow, but cites al-Ikhtiyar allowing it also for the irrevocably divorced woman. Ta'rid is defined as using a word that has both a literal and an alluded meaning, where the literal meaning is intended but the context hints at a proposal. The text then adds general rules: a woman must perform her 'iddah in the home where she was residing at the time of separation, death, or divorce. Exceptions are if she is expelled by the owner, fears loss of property or the house collapsing, or cannot pay the rent. In such cases she may move to another home. Finally, for an irrevocably divorced woman, there must be a screen (sutrah) between her and her ex-husband to prevent seclusion, as she is now a non-mahram. For a revocable divorce, a screen is not required because the husband has the right to take her back at any time, though it is recommended.

Key Terms
المعتدة (al-mu'taddah): A woman observing the waiting period ('iddah) after divorce or death of her husband.
الرجعي (al-raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new contract.
البائن (al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife during 'iddah; she becomes a non-mahram immediately.
الملوين (al-maluwayn): Dual of 'mala', meaning day and night; the period during which a widow may go out for necessities.
التعريض (al-ta'rid): Indirect proposal of marriage using ambiguous words that hint at marriage without explicit declaration.
السترة (al-sutrah): A screen or barrier to prevent seclusion between a woman and a non-mahram man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce
C) A widow in 'iddah
D) A divorced woman who has no maintenance

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'iddah residence is strict for divorced women but allows widows to go out, and ta'rid is permissible under specific conditions.

The Obligation to Observe Iddah in the Home and Its Exceptions
Ruling on iddah residence with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a woman must observe iddah in her home, list the valid excuses for leaving, and explain the evidence from the hadith of Fatimah bint Qais.

Core Idea
Iddah in the home is obligatory; leaving is haram unless a genuine excuse exists.

This lesson explores the foundational rule that a divorced or widowed woman must remain in her marital home during iddah. The commentator clarifies exceptions permitted by the Sunnah, such as eviction, fear for property, or inability to pay rent. We examine the hadith of Fatimah bint Qais as proof that leaving is allowed for necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement that a woman must observe iddah in her home at the time of separation, death, or divorce.
The text says 'She observes iddah in her home' – this is the default ruling.
2
List the exceptions
Identify the three exceptions mentioned: eviction, fear of loss or collapse, and inability to pay rent.
These are cases where leaving the home is permitted.
3
Understand the principle behind exceptions
The commentator states that going out is forbidden except for a valid excuse. The hadith of Fatimah bint Qais is cited as evidence.
The Prophet's permission shows that leaving is allowed when staying poses hardship.
4
Analyze the dalil
Examine the hadith reference: the Prophet allowed Fatimah to leave her home because she was in a frightening place or had difficulty with the husband's family.
The dalil is ahad (single-narrator) but its meaning is clear (qat'i dalala) for permissibility.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a hypothetical situation (e.g., the house is unsafe), determine if the woman may leave and why.
Use the exception list to justify.

She observes iddah in her home at the time of separation, death, and divorce unless she is evicted, or fears loss of her property or collapse of the building, or cannot find rent for the house. There must be a screen between them in the case of a ba'in divorce, so she needs to go out unlike the divorced woman because maintenance is due to her. [1] His saying: 'at the time' is connected to 'her home'; the summary: it is obligatory upon her to observe iddah in the home she was in at the time of divorce, death, or separation in a void marriage. [2] His saying: 'unless...' etc.; the principle here is that it is forbidden for her to go out except for an excuse that necessitates going out. For this reason the Prophet allowed Fatimah bint Qais to go out from her home when she became ba'in, and ordered her to observe iddah in another house; because she was in a frightening desolate place, as related by Bukhari from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her; or because she was ill-mannered and foul-tongued, unable to stay with the husband's family, as related by Abu Dawood. I have elaborated on this topic in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad (3). [3] His saying: 'she is evicted' – passive feminine from 'ikhrāj'; i.e., the husband or his heirs evict her from her house, even though that is forbidden for them. [4] His saying: 'There must be a screen' – with a dammah on the seen – i.e., something that screens like a wall and the like, because seclusion with her is forbidden. [5] His saying: 'in the ba'in' – and in the raj'i, a screen is not necessary because the connection is not completely severed, and he can take her back at any time he wishes, but it is recommended and that he should not enter upon her except with her permission. (1) i.e., if she fears the house she lives in will collapse, or she cannot find the rent for this house. (2) Lest he seclude himself with a foreign woman, and its implication is that the barrier prevents the forbidden seclusion. See al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:321). (3) al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad (2:535). Book of Divorce, Chapter of Iddah [2] And if the house is narrow for them both, the preferred view is that he should go out, similarly if he is immoral, and it is good for her to place between them a woman capable of preventing. And if he makes her ba'in, or she dies in a journey, and between her and her city there is not a travel distance, she returns, even if that... [1] His saying: 'the preferred view' – meaning that it is more preponderant, not the opposite of obligation, for his going out in such a case is obligatory because her staying until the end of iddah is obligatory. Thus in al-Fath. (2) His saying: 'similarly if he is immoral' – i.e., if the husband is immoral, not trusted, then the preferred view is that he goes out. [3] His saying: 'if he makes her ba'in' – i.e., makes her ba'in by three or less making her ba'in. [4] His saying: 'in a journey' – connected to both making ba'in and travel, meaning if he traveled with his wife to another city and divorced her with a ba'in divorce or the husband died and iddah becomes obligatory upon her in the journey. [5] His saying: 'and there is not' – i.e., the distance is not equal to a travel distance between the place where she was divorced or he died, in short: the place where iddah became obligatory and between her city from which she left, and it is more general than whether it is her original home or his original home or a residence for either, and more general than whether it is a conventional city or a village. In short, the place where she was residing before this travel. [6] His saying: 'she returns' – i.e., it is obligatory upon her to return to her city and observe iddah in her place of residence, whether she has a mahram or not, because traveling less than the duration of a journey is permissible for women without a mahram, and this is not the start of going out such that it becomes forbidden due to iddah, but rather it is a continuation of the first going out. [7] His saying: 'even if that' – i.e., the travel distance, meaning that between the place where iddah became obligatory and her city, and likewise between her destination and that place, and between what the husband intended to reach by traveling, the distance is three days and nights or more, whether they are equal in distance...
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It explains the ruling that a woman must observe iddah in the house where she was residing at the time of separation, death, or divorce. The commentator clarifies exceptions: she may leave if evicted, fears for property, or cannot afford rent. The narration of Fatimah bint Qais is cited as evidence that leaving is permissible with an excuse. The screen (sutra) is obligatory for a ba'in divorce to prevent seclusion, but not required in raj'i divorce. If the house is narrow or the husband is immoral, it is preferred for him to leave. If divorce or death occurs during travel, she must return to her place of residence if the distance is less than a travel distance (three days). This is not considered a new departure but a continuation.

Key Terms
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband.
سترة (sutra): A physical barrier like a wall between the man and woman in ba'in divorce to prevent seclusion.
بائن (bā'in): A divorce that severs the marriage tie completely, making remarriage impossible without a new contract.
رجعي (raj‘ī): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back the wife during iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Sutra Requirement and Travel Rules During Iddah
Details on sutra and travel rulings during iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a screen (sutra) is required between spouses during iddah and state the ruling for a woman who begins iddah while traveling.

Core Idea
A screen is wajib in ba'in divorce but not raj'i; if iddah starts during travel, she returns home if the distance is less than travel distance.

This lesson covers two specific scenarios: the requirement of a physical screen between ex-spouses during iddah, and the situation when a woman’s iddah begins during a journey. The commentator explains that in ba'in divorce a screen is obligatory to prevent seclusion, while in raj'i it is only recommended. For travel, the woman must return to her residence if the remaining distance is less than three days, and this is not considered a new departure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for sutra
Find the statement that a screen (sutra) is necessary in ba'in divorce.
The text says 'There must be a screen between them in the ba'in'.
2
Contrast with raj'i
Note the commentary: in raj'i, a screen is not necessary because the marriage tie is not completely severed.
The commentator says 'in raj'i the screen is not necessary'.
3
Understand the travel scenario
Identify the case: if a woman is divorced or widowed during a journey, she must return to her place of residence if the distance to that place is less than a travel distance (three days).
The text says 'she returns' – it is obligatory.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The commentary explains this is not a new departure but a continuation, so it is allowed even without a mahram.
This relies on the principle that short travel (less than three days) is permissible for women.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a scenario where a woman is divorced in a city 2 days away from her home, determine the ruling.
She must return because the distance is less than travel distance.

[3] قوله : تُخرج ؛ مجهول مؤنّث من الإخراج ؛ أي يُخرجها الزوج أو ورثته من بيتها، وإن كان ذلك حراماً عليهم. [5] قوله : وليس ؛ أي ليست المسافة بقدر السفر بين المكان التي طلقت فيه أو مات، وبالجملة : المكانُ الذي وجبت فيه العدّة وبين مصرها التي خرجت منه، وهو أعم من أن يكون وطنها الأصلي، أو يكون وطنه الأصلي، أو يكون وطن الإقامة لأحدهما، وأعم من أن يكون مصراً اصطلاحياً أو قرية، وبالجملة : المكان الذي كانت مقيمة فيه قبل هذا السفر. She observes iddah in her home at the time of separation, death, and divorce unless she is evicted, or fears loss of her property or collapse of the building, or cannot find rent for the house. There must be a screen between them in the case of a ba'in divorce, so she needs to go out unlike the divorced woman because maintenance is due to her. [1] His saying: 'at the time' is connected to 'her home'; the summary: it is obligatory upon her to observe iddah in the home she was in at the time of divorce, death, or separation in a void marriage. [2] His saying: 'unless...' etc.; the principle here is that it is forbidden for her to go out except for an excuse that necessitates going out. For this reason the Prophet allowed Fatimah bint Qais to go out from her home when she became ba'in, and ordered her to observe iddah in another house; because she was in a frightening desolate place, as related by Bukhari from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her; or because she was ill-mannered and foul-tongued, unable to stay with the husband's family, as related by Abu Dawood. I have elaborated on this topic in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad (3). [3] His saying: 'she is evicted' – passive feminine from 'ikhrāj'; i.e., the husband or his heirs evict her from her house, even though that is forbidden for them. [4] His saying: 'There must be a screen' – with a dammah on the seen – i.e., something that screens like a wall and the like, because seclusion with her is forbidden. [5] His saying: 'in the ba'in' – and in the raj'i, a screen is not necessary because the connection is not completely severed, and he can take her back at any time he wishes, but it is recommended and that he should not enter upon her except with her permission. (1) i.e., if she fears the house she lives in will collapse, or she cannot find the rent for this house. (2) Lest he seclude himself with a foreign woman, and its implication is that the barrier prevents the forbidden seclusion. See al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:321). (3) al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad (2:535). Book of Divorce, Chapter of Iddah [2] And if the house is narrow for them both, the preferred view is that he should go out, similarly if he is immoral, and it is good for her to place between them a woman capable of preventing. And if he makes her ba'in, or she dies in a journey, and between her and her city there is not a travel distance, she returns, even if that... [1] His saying: 'the preferred view' – meaning that it is more preponderant, not the opposite of obligation, for his going out in such a case is obligatory because her staying until the end of iddah is obligatory. Thus in al-Fath. (2) His saying: 'similarly if he is immoral' – i.e., if the husband is immoral, not trusted, then the preferred view is that he goes out. [3] His saying: 'if he makes her ba'in' – i.e., makes her ba'in by three or less making her ba'in. [4] His saying: 'in a journey' – connected to both making ba'in and travel, meaning if he traveled with his wife to another city and divorced her with a ba'in divorce or the husband died and iddah becomes obligatory upon her in the journey. [5] His saying: 'and there is not' – i.e., the distance is not equal to a travel distance between the place where she was divorced or he died, in short: the place where iddah became obligatory and between her city from which she left, and it is more general than whether it is her original home or his original home or a residence for either, and more general than whether it is a conventional city or a village. In short, the place where she was residing before this travel. [6] His saying: 'she returns' – i.e., it is obligatory upon her to return to her city and observe iddah in her place of residence, whether she has a mahram or not, because traveling less than the duration of a journey is permissible for women without a mahram, and this is not the start of going out such that it becomes forbidden due to iddah, but rather it is a continuation of the first going out. [7] His saying: 'even if that' – i.e., the travel distance, meaning that between the place where iddah became obligatory and her city, and likewise between her destination and that place, and between what the husband intended to reach by traveling, the distance is three days and nights or more, whether they are equal in distance...
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It explains the ruling that a woman must observe iddah in the house where she was residing at the time of separation, death, or divorce. The commentator clarifies exceptions: she may leave if evicted, fears for property, or cannot afford rent. The narration of Fatimah bint Qais is cited as evidence that leaving is permissible with an excuse. The screen (sutra) is obligatory for a ba'in divorce to prevent seclusion, but not required in raj'i divorce. If the house is narrow or the husband is immoral, it is preferred for him to leave. If divorce or death occurs during travel, she must return to her place of residence if the distance is less than a travel distance (three days). This is not considered a new departure but a continuation.

Key Terms
سترة (sutra): A physical barrier like a wall between the man and woman in ba'in divorce to prevent seclusion.
بائن (bā'in): A divorce that severs the marriage tie completely, making remarriage impossible without a new contract.
رجعي (raj‘ī): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back the wife during iddah without a new contract.
مسيرة سفر (masīrat safar): A travel distance, typically three days and nights in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is divorced with a ba'in divorce while on a trip 2 days from her home. What must she do?
A) She must stay in the trip location and observe iddah there
B) She must return home because the distance is less than travel distance
C) She may choose to stay or return
D) She must return only if she has a mahram

Key Takeaway
In ba'in divorce a screen is wajib, but not in raj'i; if iddah begins during travel, the woman must return home if the distance is less than three days.

Place of 'Iddah: Obligation to Stay Home and Travel Exceptions
Commentary explaining rulings on 'iddah location when dwelling is narrow or travel occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a woman in 'iddah regarding her location, identify the exceptions for travel, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding leaving the city with a mahram.

Core Idea
The 'iddah woman must stay in her home; only necessity (desert or travel distance) allows her to move.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses a practical scenario: where must a woman observe 'iddah when she is divorced or widowed during travel? The commentary clarifies that her primary obligation is to stay in her home, but when she is away from her city, specific rules apply depending on the distance. It also presents an important intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether she may leave the city with a mahram due to hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base obligation
A woman in 'iddah must remain in her home; leaving is prohibited unless a valid excuse (necessary) exists.
The base ruling is from the Quran and Sunnah.
2
Distinguish travel scenarios by distance
If the place where 'iddah becomes due is less than a travel distance (three days) from her home city, she must return (wajib). If it is a travel distance or more, she may choose between returning or going to the intended destination.
'Returning is better' so that 'iddah is observed in the husband's city.
3
Recognize the exception for necessity
If she is in a desert where she cannot observe 'iddah, she may move to a place where she can, even without a mahram.
This is due to the principle that necessity permits prohibitions.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow leaving the city with a mahram due to hardship; Abu Hanifa prohibits leaving even with a mahram because the prohibition is absolute.
The commentary upholds Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
In fatwa, the woman in 'iddah may not leave the city even with a mahram; only if she is in a desert or facing extreme necessity may she move.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. And if the dwelling is constricted for them, the preferable (act) is that he leaves, and likewise if he is corrupt, and it is good that she places between them a woman capable of preventing. (Meaning: that there be between them a trustworthy woman to prevent). And if he divorces her irrevocably, or he dies from her while traveling, and between her and her city there is not a travel distance, she returns. And if that (distance) [Comment: 'the preferable' means it is more preponderant, not opposite obligation, for his leaving then is obligatory because her staying until completion of 'iddah is obligatory. Likewise in 'al-Fath'. 'Likewise if he is corrupt' meaning if the husband is corrupt and not safe from him, then the preferable is that he leaves. 'And if he divorces her irrevocably' meaning he made her irrevocably divorced by three or less that is irrevocable. 'In travel' is related to both the irrevocable divorce and travel, meaning if he traveled with his wife to another country and divorced her irrevocably, or the husband died and the 'iddah became obligatory during travel. 'And not' meaning the distance is not equal to travel distance, between the place where she was divorced or he died, and in summary: the place where 'iddah became obligatory and her city from which she left, and it is more general than being her original home or his original home or the residence of either, and more general than being a city conventionally or a village, and in summary: the place where she was residing before this travel. 'Every side' and one does not consider villages and cities on the right and left because they are neither her home nor destination, so considering them would harm her. 'She is given choice' in the passive form from 'takhyeer', meaning it is permissible for her to return to her city or go to her destination, because since she cannot stay in that place, and the home and destination are equal in requiring travel, she is given choice, so she chooses whichever she wishes according to her interests. 'Whether she has a guardian or not' meaning here the guardian is the mahram with whom travel is permissible, and she is permitted to travel here to any direction she wishes without a mahram due to necessity, and necessities permit prohibitions, and therefore if this occurs in a place where she can observe 'iddah, she is not permitted to leave it. 'Returning is better' meaning better than going to the destination, so that the 'iddah occurs in the place of the husband and his city. 'She observes 'iddah' meaning it is obligatory for her to observe 'iddah there, whether she has a mahram or not. That is (the ruling). And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said to him: If she has a mahram, there is no harm in her leaving the city with him before she does not observe 'iddah, because the act of going out itself is permissible to repel the harm of estrangement and loneliness, and waiting for the 'iddah woman, though it is obligatory in her home, yet moving is permissible with an excuse like fear of loss of property or life, as has passed, and this i.e., estrangement and loneliness is an excuse permitting going out, and the prohibition is only for travel, and it has been removed by the mahram. And to him (Abu Hanifa): The woman in 'iddah is prohibited from going out absolutely, whether to a travel distance or less, and she is permitted to go out only if she is in the desert because she cannot observe 'iddah there. As for in the city, since she can observe 'iddah there, her going out is forbidden even if she has a mahram, because what...
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya, explaining rulings related to the 'iddah woman's location. The commentator begins by addressing a scenario where the dwelling is narrow, ruling that it is preferable for the husband to leave, and it is good for the wife to place a trustworthy woman between them. If the husband is corrupt and untrustworthy, the same applies. Then the discussion turns to 'iddah during travel: If a husband gives an irrevocable divorce or dies while traveling, and the place where 'iddah becomes due is less than a travel distance (three days) from her home city, she must return to her city to observe 'iddah, even without a mahram, because traveling less than a travel distance is permissible for women alone. If the distance is a travel distance or more (from both her home and the intended destination), she may choose either to return to her city or go to the destination, whichever is in her interest, and returning is better. This choice applies even without a mahram due to necessity. The commentator then presents an intra-madhhab dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that if she has a mahram, she may leave the city during 'iddah to avoid hardship (estrangement), because the mahram removes the prohibition of travel. However, Abu Hanifa holds that the prohibition of leaving the house during 'iddah is absolute; she may not leave the city even with a mahram, because she can observe 'iddah there. The only exception is if she is in the desert, where she cannot properly observe 'iddah, then she may move. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa. The rulings are based on the principle of necessity (darura) as a rational proof.

Key Terms
العدة (al-‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry, during which she must remain in her home.
بائنة (bā’inah): An irrevocable divorce after which the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
مسيرة سفر (masīrat safar): The distance that constitutes legal travel, defined as three days and nights (approximately 48 farsakh or 144 km).
ولي/محرم (walī / muḥrim): A male relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., father, brother, son), who can accompany a woman on travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a woman is divorced irrevocably during travel, and the distance between her location and her home city is a travel distance (three days or more), what are her options?
A) She must return to her home city.
B) She may choose between returning to her home city or going to the intended destination.
C) She must stay where she is.
D) She may only go to the intended destination.

Key Takeaway
A woman in 'iddah must stay in her home; if she is away, she must return if less than travel distance, or may choose if travel distance, and necessity allows moving from a desert.

Choice of Location for 'Iddah During Travel
Explains rulings for a woman in 'iddah stranded between home and destination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a woman in 'iddah stranded between home and destination, apply the distinction between necessity and convenience, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi disagreement on leaving a city with a mahram.

Core Idea
Necessity permits choice; but once home is reachable, iddah is fixed.

What happens when a woman is divorced or widowed while traveling, and she is stranded somewhere between her home and her destination? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah unpacks the nuanced Hanafi rules. You will learn when she must return, when she may choose, and why Abu Hanifa and his two students disagreed on leaving a city with a mahram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's situation
Determine if the woman is in a non-residential place (not her home or destination) and whether she is within a travel distance of either.
Travel distance (masirat safar) in Hanafi fiqh is about three days journey.
2
Apply the rule of compulsory return
If her current location is less than a travel distance from her home, she must return to observe iddah there.
3
Determine choice when equidistant
If her location is a travel distance from both home and destination, she may choose either, but returning is better (ahmad). This applies even without a mahram due to necessity.
Al-Sarakhsi adds: she chooses the nearer.
4
Handle less-than-travel distances
If both directions are less than a travel distance, she may choose (or choose the nearer). If one is a travel distance and the other less, she goes to the nearer.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
If she is in a city where she can observe iddah, Abu Hanifa prohibits leaving even with a mahram. The Sahibayn allow leaving with a mahram due to loneliness. Understand each proof and the thamarah al-khilaf.
The author favors Abu Hanifa's view, as per al-Hidayah.

اعلم أن الإبانة أو الموت في السفر: إما في غير موضع الإقامة، فإن لم تكن بينها وبين مصرها الذي خرجت منه مسيرة سفر، رجعت، وإن كانت تلك من كل جانب خُيّرت بين الرجوع والتوجه إلى المقصد سواء كان معها ولي أو لا، لكن الرجوع أولى؛ ليكون الاعتداد في منزل الزوج. وذكر الإمام السرخسي: تختار أقربهما. بقي هنا قسمان: أحدهما: ما إذا كان من كل جانب أقل من مسيرة سفر ينبغي أن تخير، وعلى قياس قول السرخسي تختار أقربهما. والثاني: ما إذا كان بينهما وبين مصرها مسيرة سفر، وبينها وبين المقصد أقل تتوجه إلى المقصد. [من كل جانب خُيّرت معها ولي أو لا، والعود أحمد، وإن كانت في مصر تعتد] قوله: كل جانب؛ ولا يعتبر ما في الميمنة والميسرة من القرى والأمصار؛ لأنه ليس وطناً لها ولا مقصداً، ففي اعتباره إضرار بها. [قوله: خيرت] بصيغة المجهول من التخيير أي يجوز لها أن تعود إلى مصرها، وأن تذهب إلى مقصدها؛ لأنه لما لم يكن لها أن تمكث في ذلك المكان والوطن، والمقصد متساويان في لزوم السفر، خُيّرت، فتختار أيهما شاءت حسب مصالحها. [قوله: معها ولي أو لا] المراد بالولي هاهنا هو المحرم الذي يجوز سفرها به، وإنما جاز لها السفر هاهنا إلى أي جهة شاءت بدون المحرم؛ لعروض الضرورة، والضرورات تبيح المخطورات؛ ولذا لو وقع ذلك في موضع تتمكن من الاعتداد هناك فلا يباح لها الخروج منه. [قوله: أحمد] أي أفضل من الرواح على المقصد، وذلك لتقع العدة في مكان الزوج ومصره. [قوله: تعتد] أي يجب عليها أن تعتد هناك، سواء كان معها محرم أو لا، هذا عنده. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد له: إن كان معها محرم فلا بأس أن تخرج من المصر معه قبل أن لا تعتد؛ لأن نفس الخروج مباح؛ دفعاً لأذى الغربة، ووحشة الوحدة، والتربص على المعتدة وإن كان واجباً في منزلها، لكن يجوز لها الانتقال بعذر كخوف تلف المال والنفس، كما مرّ، وهذا أي الغربة والوحشة عذر مبيح للخروج، وإنما الحرمة للسفر، وقد اندفعت بالمحرم. وله: إن المعتدة منعت عن الخروج مطلقاً، سواء كان إلى مدة السفر أو ما دونها، وإنما جاز لها الخروج إذا كانت في المفازة؛ لأنها لا تتمكن من الاعتدادِ هناك، وأما في المصر فلما تمكنت من الاعتداد به، يحرم خروجها وإن كان معها محرم، فإنّ ما يرخص بالضرورة يتقدّر بقدرها، ونفس الغربة والوحشة بدون ضمّ ضميمة، ليس بعذر قوي يباحُ الخروج. كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها. [قوله: الذي... الخ] أشار به إلى أنه ليس المراد بمصرها وطنها، بل أعم. [قوله: تختار أقربهما] إلى ذلك المكان، يعني تخرج إلى ما كان أقل مسافة بالنسبة إلى الآخر، مثلاً إن كان بين مكان وجوب عدّتها وبين مصرها مقدار أربعة أيام، وبينه وبين مقصدها ثلاثة أيام، ينبغي لها أن تذهب إلى المقصد وتعتد هناك. [قوله: ينبغي أن تخير] أي بين الخروج إلى المقصد وبين الرجوع إلى مصرها. [قوله: تتوجه إلى المقصد] لكون الخروج إليه أقل من السفر، وكون الرجوع مستلزماً للسفر، ومن ابتلي ببليتين يختار أهونها، ومن المعلوم أن الخروج أقل من السفر أهون من الخروج بقدر السفر، حتى يشترط له وجود المحرم دون الأول.
Know that divorce or death during travel: either in a non-residential place. If there is not between her and her city from which she departed a travel distance, she returns. And if that place is equidistant from all sides, she is given choice between returning and proceeding to the destination, whether she has a guardian or not, but returning is better so that the 'iddah occurs in the husband's home. Imam al-Sarakhsi mentioned: she chooses the nearer of the two. There remain two cases: one: when from every side it is less than a travel distance, she should choose, and by analogy to al-Sarakhsi's statement she chooses the nearer. The second: when between her and her city is a travel distance, and between her and the destination is less, she proceeds to the destination. [From every side she is given choice, whether she has a guardian or not, and returning is more commendable, and if she is in a city she observes 'iddah] His statement: 'every side': what is on the right and left of villages and cities is not considered, because it is neither her home nor her destination, so considering it would harm her. [His statement: 'she is given choice' in the passive voice of choosing, meaning it is permissible for her to return to her city or go to her destination; because since she cannot stay in that place, and the home and destination are equal in necessitating travel, she is given choice, so she chooses whichever she wishes according to her interests. [His statement: 'whether she has a guardian or not'] the meaning of guardian here is the mahram with whom travel is permissible for her. She is permitted to travel here to any direction she wishes without the mahram due to necessity, and necessities permit prohibited things; hence if this occurs in a place where she can observe 'iddah there, she is not permitted to leave it. [His statement: 'more commendable'] meaning better than going to the destination, so that the 'iddah takes place in the husband's home and city. [His statement: 'she observes 'iddah'] meaning she must observe 'iddah there, whether she has a mahram or not, this is according to him (Abu Hanifa). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: if she has a mahram, then there is no harm in her leaving the city with him before observing 'iddah, because the act of leaving itself is permissible to repel the harm of strangeness and loneliness, and waiting ('iddah) for the woman in 'iddah although obligatory in her home, but it is permissible for her to move due to an excuse like fear of loss of property or life, as has passed, and this (strangeness and loneliness) is an excuse that permits leaving, and the prohibition is only for travel, and it has been removed by the mahram. According to him (Abu Hanifa): the woman in 'iddah is absolutely prohibited from leaving, whether to a travel distance or less, and she is only permitted to leave if she is in a desert because she cannot observe 'iddah there. As for being in the city, since she can observe 'iddah there, her leaving is prohibited even if she has a mahram, for what is permitted by necessity is limited to its extent, and mere strangeness and loneliness without an additional factor is not a strong enough excuse to permit leaving. This is stated in al-Hidayah and its glosses. [His statement: 'which... etc.'] he indicated by this that the meaning of 'her city' is not her hometown, but more general. [His statement: 'she chooses the nearer of the two' meaning to that place, i.e., she goes to the one that is less distant relative to the other; for example, if between the place where her 'iddah is obligatory and her city is four days, and between it and her destination is three days, she should go to the destination and observe 'iddah there. [His statement: 'she should choose'] meaning between going to the destination and returning to her city. [His statement: 'she proceeds to the destination'] because going there is less than a travel distance, while returning would necessitate a travel distance, and one afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lesser; and it is known that going a distance less than travel is easier than going a full travel distance, so much so that the presence of a mahram is required for the latter but not for the former.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on Al-Wiqayah, dealing with the rules of 'iddah for a woman who becomes divorced or widowed while traveling away from both her marital home (misr) and her intended destination (maqsad). The author explains the main ruling: if her current location is less than a travel distance (masirat safar, defined elsewhere as three days or 48 miles) from her home, she must return. If the distance to both home and destination is equal (i.e., both are a travel distance away), she is given the choice to go either way, whether she has a mahram or not, but returning to the husband's home is better (ahmad) because the 'iddah should ideally be observed there. If she is in a city (misr) and can observe 'iddah there, she must stay and cannot leave, even with a mahram. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ: they allow her to leave the city with a mahram before starting her 'iddah if she fears loneliness or strangeness, considering these as valid excuses. The passage then discusses two further cases: (1) if the distance from all sides is less than a travel distance, she should choose (according to the main view) or choose the nearer (according to al-Sarakhsi); (2) if one side is a travel distance and the other less, she goes to the nearer, as it is the lesser evil. The commentary clarifies key terms like 'kulli janib' (every side) meaning directions in which she has a home or destination, not just any village or city, and 'wali' meaning mahram. The necessity (darurah) of being stranded permits what is normally prohibited, like traveling without a mahram, but only to the extent needed.

Key Terms
مصر (misr): The city or town that is the woman's marital home (or her original home from which she departed).
مقصد (maqsad): The intended destination to which she was traveling when the divorce/death occurred.
مسيرة سفر (masirat safar): The distance that constitutes a legal journey (safar), typically three days travel or about 48 miles.
ولي (wali): A mahram (unmarriageable kin) with whom travel is legally permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, can a woman in 'iddah leave a city where she can stay, if she has a mahram?
A) Yes, because she feels lonely.
B) No, because she can observe 'iddah there and necessity does not permit it.
C) Only if the distance is more than a travel duration.
D) Yes, but only to return to her husband's home.

Key Takeaway
A woman in 'iddah must observe it in her home if possible; necessity may permit a choice when stranded, but once she can observe 'iddah in a place, she must stay.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Where to Observe 'Iddah When Traveling
Explains rulings for location of 'iddah during travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on where a woman in 'iddah should observe her waiting period when she is traveling, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the distance-based rules to determine her proper location.

Core Idea
A woman in 'iddah must stay put; travel exceptions depend on distance, and within Hanafi school, the Sahibayn allow exit with a mahram.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on a brief ruling from al-Wiqayah. It addresses a practical problem: a woman who is divorced or widowed while traveling—must she return home, proceed to her destination, or stay where she is? The answer hinges on distances and on an internal Hanafi disagreement about whether she may leave her residence at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage divides the case into two broad scenarios: the woman is in a non-residence place (i.e. traveling) or she is in a residence place (a city/village). Each has different rules.
2
Apply the distance rules for non-residence
If she is in a place that is not her regular home, compare the distances to her original city and to her destination. If both are less than travel distance (safar), she may choose. If one is safar and the other less, she goes to the nearer. If both are equal, she chooses but return is preferable.
Travel distance is three days journey; if less, it is not safar.
3
Analyze the residence place scenario and the ikhtilaf
If she is in a city (or village) where she resides, she must stay unless she has a mahram. Even with a mahram, Abu Hanifa forbids leaving; the Sahibayn allow it to alleviate loneliness. This is a key intra-Hanafi difference.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.
4
Connect the two scenarios
Once the Sahibayn allow exit with a mahram, the same distance rules apply: she may go to the nearer of her options.
So the detailed rules above also apply to residence cases according to the Sahibayn.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of 'Iddah. Then she goes out with a mahram. Know that the divorce or death during travel: 1. Either in a non-residence place: if there is not between her and her city from which she left a travel distance, she returns, and if it [the distance] is equal from each side, she chooses between returning and proceeding to the destination, whether she has a guardian or not, but returning is better so that the 'iddah is in the husband's home. Imam al-Sarakhsi mentioned: she chooses the nearer of them. Two categories remain: First: if from both sides it is less than a travel distance, she should be given choice, and according to al-Sarakhsi's analogy she chooses the nearer. Second: if between her and her city is a travel distance, and between her and the destination is less, she proceeds to the destination. Necessity is permitted according to its extent, and the mere feeling of strangeness and loneliness without additional reason is not a strong excuse permitting going out. Thus in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. [1] His saying 'which... etc.': he indicated that what is meant by her city is not her homeland, but more general. [2] His saying 'she chooses the nearer': to that place, meaning she goes to whichever is less distance relative to the other, e.g. if between the place of obligation of 'iddah and her city is four days, and between it and her destination is three days, she should go to the destination and observe 'iddah there. [3] His saying 'she should be given choice': i.e. between going out to the destination and returning to her city. [4] His saying 'she proceeds to the destination': because going out to it is less than travel, while returning necessitates travel, and one afflicted with two afflictions chooses the lesser, and it is known that going out less than travel is easier than going out at a travel distance, such that the presence of a mahram is required for the latter but not for the former. (1) In al-Mabsut (6:35). (2) But al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut (6:35) said: if between her and her destination is less than a travel distance, and between her and her home likewise, then she must return to her home because as soon as she returns she becomes a resident, and if she goes forward she remains a traveler until she reaches the destination. As for a residence place, and that is what he said: if she is in a city, i.e. when he divorces her or dies, if she has no guardian with her, then [she stays] without a guardian, and if she has a guardian, then the same according to Abu Hanifa, because the going out of a woman in 'iddah is forbidden even if the distance is less than a travel period. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] it is permissible because the going out itself is permitted to alleviate the loneliness of separation; the prohibition is only for travel, and it is removed due to the presence of a guardian. Then, since going out is permitted according to them, to which direction she proceeds, then the ruling should be according to the detailed discussion that passed. [1] His saying 'and as for a residence place': it is connected to his earlier saying 'either in a non-residence place', and by the term 'residence place' he indicated that the mention of 'city' in the text is incidental, for the ruling is the same in villages: she observes 'iddah there and does not go out until the completion of the 'iddah. [2] His saying 'guardian': i.e. a mahram with whom it is permitted for her to go out and travel. [3] His saying 'because...etc.': he indicated that his ruling of observing 'iddah there is not due to the prohibition of travel without a mahram, such that it would be said that it becomes permissible when a mahram is present, or that it is permissible for her to go out if between that city and her city or destination is less than three days and nights; rather it is based on the prohibition of a woman in 'iddah leaving the house in which the 'iddah was obligated absolutely. [4] His saying 'rather the prohibition': its gist is that the prohibition of her going out here is not due to the 'iddah because of the presence of the excuse that makes it permissible; rather it is the prohibition of travel without a mahram, and it is removed by the presence of a guardian, and the answer to that has already passed. [0] His saying 'then the ruling should be according to the detailed discussion that passed': i.e. if between her and each of the homeland and destination is a travel distance, she chooses or chooses the nearer, similarly if between each of them and her is less than a travel distance, and if between her and one of them is a travel distance and between her and the other is less, she proceeds to the lesser.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding a woman in 'iddah who is traveling. The base text states 'Then she goes out with a mahram,' but the commentary clarifies the detailed scenarios. The author begins by explaining that if a woman is divorced or widowed while traveling in a place that is not her regular residence, she must assess distances. If the distance to her original city is less than the travel distance (safar), she must return there to observe 'iddah. If both the city and her intended destination are at equal distance (both less than safar), she has a choice but returning is preferable to observe 'iddah in the husband's home. Imam al-Sarakhsi from al-Mabsut adds that she should choose the nearer of the two. Two remaining cases: first, if both distances are less than safar, she is given choice and according to Sarakhsi chooses nearer; second, if one side is safar and the other less, she goes to the nearer side. The commentary emphasizes that mere loneliness is not an excuse to leave; necessity is measured by its extent. Then the passage addresses the case where she is in a residence place (like a city). Here, if she has no mahram, she must stay. If she has a mahram, Imam Abu Hanifa says she still cannot leave because leaving the 'iddah house is forbidden in itself, regardless of distance. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow leaving with a mahram to alleviate the loneliness of separation, and then the same distance-based details apply. The marginal notes explain that the prohibition according to Abu Hanifa is not about travel without mahram but about leaving the 'iddah house absolutely. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the view of the Sahibayn in later fatwa, but the text presents both views.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband during which she cannot remarry.
سفر (safar): A journey of at least three days and nights (approximately 48 miles/77 km) that permits shortening of prayers and breaking fast. Here used as a criterion for distance.
محرم (mahram): A male relative whom a woman is permanently prohibited from marrying (e.g., father, brother, son), with whom travel is permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in 'iddah is in a non-residence place. The distance back to her city is 2 days, and the distance to her destination is 2 days as well. According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) She must return to her city because that is the husband's home.
B) She may choose between returning and proceeding, but returning is better.
C) She must proceed to the destination.
D) She must stay where she is.

Key Takeaway
A woman in 'iddah must observe it in the house where she was residing; if she is traveling, distance rules determine her options, and the Sahibayn's view (allowing exit with a mahram) is the relied-upon position.

Woman in 'Iddah: Movement with Mahram - Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on leaving 'iddah residence with mahram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a woman in 'iddah leaving her residence with a mahram and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa forbids leaving the 'iddah residence; Sahibayn permit it with a mahram.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two principal disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on a specific 'iddah question: may a woman leave her residence to travel with a mahram? The disagreement turns on the nature of the prohibition—whether it is an absolute ban on leaving the 'iddah house or a ban on traveling without a guardian. Understanding this dispute clarifies the underlying usul of 'iddah rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling: the woman may not leave her 'iddah residence even with a mahram. His reasoning is that the prohibition is on leaving the house itself, not on traveling without a guardian.
The prohibition is absolute per Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Identify the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and their position: the woman may leave with a mahram. Their reasoning distinguishes between the act of leaving (mubah) and the act of traveling without a mahram (haram).
Sahibayn see the mahram as removing the prohibition.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Trace the Sahibayn's dalil: leaving the residence is permissible to alleviate the loneliness of separation from the husband; the only prohibition is on traveling without a mahram, which is removed by the presence of the guardian.
Their proof is based on the purpose of 'iddah (waiting, not confinement).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's counter-argument: the prohibition on leaving the 'iddah residence is independent of the travel prohibition. Even with a mahram, the act of leaving the house where 'iddah is owed is forbidden.
Abu Hanifa sees no concession for mahram.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah): under Abu Hanifa, the woman must stay in her residence regardless; under the Sahibayn, she may travel with a mahram. If she may travel, the earlier rules on choosing the closer destination apply.
The difference affects real-life mobility during 'iddah.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whether a woman observing 'iddah after divorce or death may leave her residence to travel, if she has a mahram (male guardian) with her. Abu Hanifa's position, stated first, is that leaving is absolutely forbidden, even with a mahram and even for a short distance. His reasoning is that the prohibition is on leaving the house where 'iddah is being observed, not on traveling without a mahram. The Sahibayn, in contrast, argue that leaving itself is permissible to alleviate the loneliness of separation from the husband; the only prohibition is on traveling without a mahram, which is removed when a mahram is present. Therefore, they permit the woman to leave her residence to travel with a mahram. The commentary then explains that the earlier mentioned detail applies: if the woman needs to travel, she may choose the closer of two destinations (hometown or intended destination) if both are within travel distance, or if only one is within travel distance, she must go to that one.

Key Terms
موضع الإقامة (mawdi' al-iqāmah): The place where a woman is residing when 'iddah begins; she must observe 'iddah there and not leave.
ولي (walī): A male guardian (mahram) with whom it is permissible for a woman to travel.
عدة (‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.

C) They argue that 'iddah is not required to be observed in any specific place.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether the prohibition in 'iddah is on leaving the residence absolutely or only on traveling without a mahram, with different practical consequences.

Full Dowry for Conditional Divorce with Lineage Establishment
Commentary on dowry ruling when conditional divorce triggers at marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on full mahr when a conditional divorce coincides with marriage and lineage is established, and apply the principle of caution in establishing lineage to justify the ruling.

Core Idea
Full dowry is due when lineage is established, even if divorce immediately follows marriage, because the law presumes consummation before divorce.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a nuanced ruling: if a man says 'if I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, and she gives birth to a child within six months, she is entitled to the full mahr, not half. The reasoning hinges on the principle that lineage is established with utmost caution (al-nasab yuhtatu fi ithbatihi), allowing the law to consider rare possibilities of intercourse coinciding with the marriage contract. Let's walk through the argument and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that when a conditional divorce triggers upon marriage and a child is born within six months, the wife is entitled to full mahr, despite the divorce occurring at the moment of marriage.
This contrasts with the default rule that divorce before consummation gives half mahr.
2
Recognize the Objection
An objector argues that two mahr payments should be due: one for the marriage contract and one for intercourse. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing the timing of intercourse relative to the contract.
The objection arises from analogy with marrying during intercourse, where two payments are due.
3
Understand the Principle
The key principle is 'al-nasab yuhtatu fi ithbatihi' (lineage is to be established cautiously). This allows the law to assume that intercourse occurred at the moment of the marriage contract, even if unlikely, to preserve the child's legitimacy.
This principle overrides the default that divorce before consummation halves the mahr.
4
Apply the Scenarios
The text provides three scenarios where intercourse could coincide with the marriage contract: simultaneous intercourse, appointment of agents, and fuduli marriage. These illustrate the legal possibility of concurrent consummation.
These scenarios are hypothetical but legally sufficient to establish lineage and full mahr.

[٢]قوله: نكحها؛ الصواب أنكحاها، يعني أنكح الوكيلان تلك المرأة بذلك الرجل في غير مجلسهما بحضرة الشهود.
His saying: 'and her mahr' means the full mahr, even if divorce before intercourse would reduce it to half, because by the establishment of lineage he is legally considered to have had intercourse, thus the divorce is after intercourse and the full mahr becomes obligatory. If you say: It should be obligatory to give two mahr: a mahr for intercourse and a mahr for marriage, as in the case of marrying a woman while he is having intercourse with her – there, a named mahr is obligatory for the marriage and an 'uqr (dower for intercourse) is obligatory. I say: In that case, the contract is concurrent with intercourse, and the consummation precedes the marriage, therefore two mahr are obligatory. Not so here, because here it is possible that intercourse occurs at the time of marriage as you will come to know. [2] His saying: 'Because it is not unlikely... etc.' This is a presentation explaining the reasoning of the issue, such that the objection raised here is repelled, which is that the establishment of lineage for the child of a married woman is dependent on the establishment of intercourse and its possibility through marriage. Indeed, unlawful intercourse does not establish lineage. In our case: When he conditioned divorce upon marriage and then married her, the divorce fell upon her as soon as he married her, so intercourse is not conceivable here except after divorce, and that (post-divorce intercourse) does not establish lineage, because there is no 'iddah without being entered upon, such that it could be considered a return (to marriage).
Then he said: 'and both appointed agents for the marriage,' so the two agents married her on a specific night, and the husband had intercourse with her that same night, and conception occurred. The summary of the answer: There is no doubt that when he conditioned divorce upon marriage, the divorce occurs after the completion of the marriage, not concurrent with it, as the conditioned (divorce) follows the condition (marriage), and there is necessarily a time between them, no matter how little, and intercourse concurrent with the marriage is possible here. Thus, intercourse occurred before divorce and suffices to establish lineage. 
His saying: 'and both appointed agents for the marriage... etc.' They have mentioned scenarios for imagining intercourse at the time of the contract. Among them: It is possible that he married her while he was on top of her having intercourse with her, and people heard their speech, and ejaculation coincided with the completion of the marriage. Divorce does not occur except after the completion of the condition, and the removal of the marriage bed is the legal effect of divorce, so conception occurred before the removal of the bed necessarily, so lineage is established. Even though this is rare, lineage is guarded cautiously in its establishment, so it must be built upon this rarity. Thus in al-'Inayah. 
(1)
Among them: What the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned. Its summary: It is possible that both the man and the woman appointed agents to marry them to each other on a specific night, and he had intercourse with her that night, and it is not known whether the ejaculation occurred before the contract of the agents or after, so it is attributed to concurrency out of precaution and rectification, and as a support for what is legally sound.
Among them: That he marries her in the presence of witnesses, and the contracting party from her side is a third party (fuduli), and the contract is completed by her consent during the act of intercourse. In short: The establishment of lineage is dependent on the marriage bed (firash), and it (the firash) is established concurrently with the marriage that is concurrent with conception, so conception occurs while she is a marriage bed, thus lineage is established.
[2] His saying: 'nikahaha' (he married her); the correct [reading] is 'ankahaha' (he married her to him), meaning the two agents married that woman to that man outside their meeting place in the presence of witnesses.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the text of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling when a man says to a woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced.' The commentator begins by noting that in such a case, if she later gives birth to a child within six months of the marriage, the child's lineage is established to the husband, which means he is legally considered to have consummated the marriage before the divorce took effect. Consequently, she is entitled to the full dowry (mahr), not half. The commentator then anticipates an objection: why not two separate payments—one for the marriage and one for intercourse? He answers by distinguishing this case from one where a man marries a woman while already having intercourse with her; there, two payments are due because intercourse preceded the contract. Here, however, it is possible that intercourse occurred simultaneously with the marriage contract itself. The main challenge is that since the divorce happens immediately upon marriage, one might think that no permissible intercourse could have occurred before the divorce. The commentator dismisses this by explaining that the divorce occurs after the completion of the marriage, not at the exact same moment—there is a minimal gap. Thus, it is possible for intercourse to occur at the very moment of the contract. The text then provides several concrete scenarios to illustrate how intercourse can coincide with the marriage contract: (1) the couple marries while already in the act of intercourse; (2) both appoint agents to perform the marriage on a specific night, and the husband has intercourse that night without knowing whether conception occurred before or after the contract—here we assume concurrency out of precaution; (3) a third-party (fuduli) marries her in the presence of witnesses, and she consents during intercourse. The core principle is that lineage is to be established with utmost caution (al-nasab yuhtatu fi ithbatihi), so even unlikely scenarios are accepted to secure the child's legitimacy. The commentator also clarifies a textual point: the phrase 'nikahaha' (he married her) should be read as 'ankahaha' (he married her to him), referring to the agents' action.

Key Terms
الفراش (al-firash): The legal status of a woman being a wife, making her a lawful bed for intercourse and meaning that any child born to her is attributed to the husband.
العقر (al-'uqr): The compensation (dower) due for intercourse, distinct from the mahr specified in the marriage contract.
العلوق (al-'uluq): Conception; the moment the woman becomes pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She gets half the dowry because divorce occurred before consummation.
B) She gets full dowry because lineage is established, implying consummation occurred.
C) She gets no dowry because the marriage was invalid.
D) She gets a deferred dowry only.

Key Takeaway
Full mahr is due when a conditional divorce coincides with marriage and a child is born within six months because the law presumes consummation to protect the child's lineage.

Establishing Lineage When Intercourse Coincides with Marriage
Commentary on scenarios where intercourse may coincide with the marriage contract in conditional divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school establishes lineage even when the exact timing of intercourse relative to the marriage contract is uncertain, and apply the precautionary principle (ihtiyat) to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Lineage is established by precaution: if intercourse could have occurred during the marital bed, the child is attributed to the husband.

In this lesson, we examine a critical issue in family law: when a divorce is made conditional upon marriage, and intercourse coincides with the contract, does the child’s lineage still hold? The Hanafi school answers with a strong principle of precaution (ihtiyat) in lineage matters, even in rare scenarios. We will explore three examples from the classical text that illustrate how the marital bed (firash) is preserved to protect the child’s parentage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
The scenario involves a conditional divorce: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.' The husband marries and has intercourse simultaneously or near-simultaneously. The question is whether the child's lineage is established if conception occurs during that ambiguous window.
The conditioned (divorce) follows the condition (marriage) with a temporal gap, however small.
2
Understand the principle of ihtiyat
Lineage is a fundamental right that requires precaution. When there is doubt about the timing of intercourse relative to the marital bed, the Hanafi school resolves the doubt in favor of establishing lineage.
This is based on istihsan and the maxim that lineage is preserved wherever possible.
3
Examine the three scenarios
The text presents three cases: (1) direct marriage with intercourse at the exact moment of contract, (2) both parties appoint agents and intercourse occurs the same night with unknown timing, (3) marriage by a third party (fuduli) with consent during intercourse. In each, the marital bed is deemed present at conception.
Even rare possibilities are considered because lineage requires extreme precaution.
4
Apply the ruling to the revocable divorce case
The passage extends the same principle: a child born to a woman in revocable divorce (raj'i) is attributed to the husband even if born after more than two years, unless she admits the iddah ended.
This shows the breadth of precaution in lineage matters.

Source Text

كتاب الطلاق باب ثبوت النسب والحضانة
وكلا بالنكاح، فالوكيلان نكحها في ليلة معينة ، والزوج وطئها في تلكِ الليلة ، ووجد العلوق وحاصل الجواب : إنه لا شبهة في أنه لما علق الطلاق بالنكاح وقع الطلاق بعد تمام النكاح ، لا مقارناً به ؛ إذ المشروط يعقب الشرط ، ويكون بينهما زمان لا محالة وإن قل، والوطء مقارناً للنكاح ممكن هاهنا، فيكون الوطء قبل الطلاق، ويكون كافياً في ثبوت النسب. 11 قوله : وكلا بالنكاح ...الخ ؛ قد ذكروا التصوّر الوطء حالة العقد صوراً : منها : إنّه يمكن أن يكون تزوجها، وهو على بطنها يُخالطها، والناس يسمعون كلامهما، ويكون الإنزال قد وافق تمام النكاح مقارناً ، والطلاق لا يقع إلا بعد تمام الشرط، وزوالُ الفراش حُكْمُ الطلاق، فيكون العلوق حاصلاً قبل زوال الفراش ضرورة، فيثبت النسب، وهذا وإن كان نادراً لكنّ النسب يحتاط في إثباته، فيجب بناؤه على هذا النادر. كذا في (العناية)

Book of Divorce, Chapter on Establishing Lineage and Custody. And both appointed agents for marriage, so the two agents married her on a specific night, and the husband had intercourse with her that same night, and conception occurred. The summary of the answer: There is no doubt that when he made divorce conditional upon marriage, the divorce occurs after the completion of the marriage, not simultaneously with it; for the conditioned thing follows the condition, and there must be a time between them, however small. And intercourse concurrent with marriage is possible here, so the intercourse occurs before the divorce, and it is sufficient for establishing lineage. He said: "And both appointed agents for marriage, etc."; they mentioned scenarios of intercourse at the time of the contract: from them: It is possible that he married her while he was on top of her having intercourse, and people hear their words, and ejaculation coincided exactly with the completion of marriage, and divorce does not occur except after the completion of the condition, and the removal of the marital bed is the effect of divorce, so conception necessarily occurs before removal of the marital bed, thus lineage is established. And although this is rare, lineage is handled with precaution in its establishment, so it must be built on this rarity. Likewise in (al-‛Inayah). And from them: What the commentator mentioned, summarized: It is possible that both the man and the woman appointed two agents to marry them to each other on a specific night, he had intercourse with her that night, and it is not known whether ejaculation occurred before the contract of the two agents or after it, so it is construed as concurrent out of precaution and rectification, and as upholding what is valid legally. And from them: That he marries her in the presence of witnesses and a third party contractor on her behalf, and the completion of the contract occurs with her consent during intercourse. In summary: The establishment of lineage depends on the marital bed, and it is established concurrently with the marriage concurrent with conception, so conception occurs while she is a marital bed, thus lineage is established. He said: "He married her" – the correct is "they married her", meaning the two agents married that woman to that man in their absence in the presence of witnesses. And the lineage of a child of a woman in a revocable divorce is established, even if she gives birth after more than two years, as long as she does not admit that the waiting period has ended.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This is a commentary on a passage from the matn concerning the establishment of lineage when a divorce has been made conditional upon marriage. The commentator expands on the scenarios where intercourse could coincide with the marriage contract, showing that even in rare situations, lineage is confirmed out of precaution (ihtiyat). The first scenario imagines the husband marrying and immediately having intercourse, with ejaculation at the exact moment of contract completion. Since the divorce only occurs after the condition (marriage) is fulfilled, the marital bed (firash) is present at the time of conception, so the child is born into wedlock. The second scenario involves agents appointed by both parties; the exact timing of ejaculation relative to the agents' contract is unknown, so it is assumed to be concurrent to uphold the validity of the marriage and protect lineage. The third scenario involves a third party (fuduli) conducting the marriage, with the wife's consent given during intercourse. In all cases, the principle is that lineage is established because the marital bed exists at the time of conception. The commentator also corrects a textual point: the verb should be 'ankaha' (they married) rather than 'nakaha' (he married). Then a new ruling is introduced: the lineage of a child born to a woman in a revocable divorce (raj'i) is established even if birth occurs after more than two years from the divorce, unless the woman herself admits that her waiting period (iddah) has ended.

Key Terms
علوق (ʿulūq): conception or fertilization; the beginning of pregnancy
فراش (firāsh): the marital bed; the legal status of being a wife that makes any child born to her attributed to the husband
احتياط (ihtiyāṭ): precaution; a principle of preferring the more cautious ruling to protect rights or avoid harm, especially in lineage and worship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where both parties appoint agents for marriage and the husband has intercourse that same night without knowing if ejaculation occurred before or after the agents' contract, what is the ruling on the child's lineage?
A) The child's lineage is not established because the timing is uncertain.
B) The child's lineage is established because it is assumed the intercourse occurred during the marital bed out of precaution.
C) The child's lineage is established only if the wife admits to intercourse after the contract.
D) The child's lineage is established only if the two agents testify that the contract was before intercourse.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, lineage is established by precaution whenever it is possible that intercourse occurred during a valid marital bed, even if the exact timing relative to the contract is uncertain.
