⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو علّق طلاقها بولادتها فشهدت امرأة بها لم يقع
عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
ولو علق طلاقها بولادتها فشهدت امرأة بها لم يقع)(۱) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما يقع ؛ لأنَّ الولادة تثبت بشهادةِ امرأة، ثُمَّ يثبت الطلاق بالتبعية. وله : إن الولادة تثبت بشهادة امرأة ضرورة، فيقدر بقدرها، فلا يتعدى إلى الطلاق، وهو ليس تبعاً لها ؛ لأن كلا منهما يوجد بدون الآخر. [1] قوله : ولو علّق...الخ ؛ يعني إذا قال لامرأة : إن نكحتك فولدت فأنت طالق ، فنكحها وولدت بعده فأنكر الزوج الولادة، وشهدت قابلة بها لم يقع الطلاق بهذه الشهادة، وإن كان يثبت النسب إذا كانت الولادة لستة أشهر فأكثر، وعندهما يقعُ الطلاق.
(٢) قوله : لأن الولادة ...الخ حاصله : إنَّ الولادة تثبت بشهادة القابلة، والطلاق وإن كان لا يثبت بشهادة امرأةٍ واحدة، لكنّه هاهنا معلّق بالولادة، فيكون كلازمه، فيثبت بثبوتها ، وكم من شيء لا يثبت بشيءٍ قصداً و يثبت به تبعاً. (۳) قوله : وله ؛ أي لأبي حنيفة ، وحاصله : إن ثبوت الولادة بشهادة امرأة واحدة إنّما هو للضرورة، وهي أنَّ تلك الحالة مما لا يحضر فيها الرجال، وأكثر ما تحضر عند الولادة القابلة، فلو تم يعتبر قولها ، لزم الحرجُ العظيم ، ولذا قال الزهري : مضت السنة أن تجوز شهادة النساء فيما لا يطلع عليه غيرهن (۲)، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، والثابت بالضرورة يتقدّر بقدرها ، فلا يتعدّى ذلك إلى غيرها، فلا يثبت الطلاق بمثل هذه الشهادة.
6
(٤) قوله : وهو ليس ...الخ ؛ جواب عما قالاء من أن الطلاق يثبت في هذه الصورة تبعاً للولادة، وحاصله : إن الطلاق ليس تبعاً لها، فإنّ كلا منهما يوجد بدون الآخر ، فقد يوجد الطلاق بدون الولادة، وقد توجد الولادة بدونه إذا لم يكن معلقاً عليها، وما هذا شأنه لا يكون تبعاً.
(۱) بشرط عدم إقرار الزوج بالحمل ، وعدم كون الحبل ظاهراً. ينظر : «البناية»(٤ : ٨٢٩).
(۲) في مصنف ابن شيبة (٤ : ٣٢٩)، وغيره.
And if he suspends her divorce upon her giving birth, and a woman testifies to it, [the divorce] does not occur. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [companions], it occurs, because childbirth is established by the testimony of a woman, then divorce is established by [its] dependency. For him [Abu Hanifa]: Childbirth is established by the testimony of a woman out of necessity, so it is limited to its extent, thus it does not extend to divorce. And it [divorce] is not dependent on it [childbirth], because each of them exists without the other. [1] His statement: 'and if he suspends...' etc. means if he said to a woman: 'If I marry you and you give birth, you are divorced', then he married her and she gave birth afterwards, and the husband denied the birth, and a midwife testified to it, divorce does not occur by this testimony, although lineage is established if the birth is at six months or more. According to the two [companions], divorce occurs. (2) His statement: 'because childbirth...' etc. summary: Childbirth is established by the testimony of the midwife, and divorce, although not established by the testimony of a single woman, is here suspended on childbirth, so it becomes like its necessary consequence, thus it is established by its establishment. Many a thing is not established by something directly but is established by it as a consequence. (3) His statement: 'and for him' i.e. for Abu Hanifa, summary: The establishment of childbirth by the testimony of a single woman is only out of necessity, that this situation is one where men are not present, and mostly only the midwife is present at birth. If her statement were not considered, great hardship would result. Therefore al-Zuhri said: 'The practice has passed that women's testimony is permissible in matters that only they can see' (recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba). What is established by necessity is limited to its extent, so it does not extend to other than it, thus divorce is not established by such testimony. (4) His statement: 'and it is not...' etc. is a response to what they said that divorce is established in this image as a consequence of childbirth. Summary: Divorce is not a consequence of it, for each exists without the other. Divorce may exist without childbirth, and childbirth may exist without it if not suspended on it. What is like this is not a consequence. (1) On condition that the husband does not acknowledge the pregnancy and that the pregnancy is not apparent. See: al-Binaya (4:829). (2) In Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (4:329) and others.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a man who suspends divorce on his wife's childbirth, then a single woman (midwife) testifies to the birth while the husband denies it. The ruling splits between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The author opens by stating the matn: according to Abu Hanifa, the divorce does not occur with the testimony of one woman; according to them, it does occur. The author then explains the reasons: The two companions argue that childbirth is established by the testimony of one woman (as in lineage cases), and since divorce is suspended on it, it follows as a necessary consequence. Abu Hanifa responds that childbirth is only established by such testimony out of necessity (as private matters), so its legal effect is confined to its immediate purpose (like lineage) and does not extend to divorce. Moreover, divorce is not truly dependent on childbirth because each can occur independently. The author then elaborates on the commentary: the matn's phrase is explained, the two companions' reasoning is stated (that many things are established as consequences), and Abu Hanifa's argument is supported by the hadith from al-Zuhri about women's testimony in private matters. The principle 'what is established by necessity is limited to its extent' (al-thabit bil-darura yuqaddar bi-qadriha) is key. The author also refutes the notion of dependency, emphasizing that divorce and childbirth are separable. The footnote clarifies that this ruling applies only if the husband does not acknowledge the pregnancy and it is not apparent. This is a critical refinement of the sharh, drilling down into the exact legal reasoning.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق (ta‘līq al-ṭalāq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce on a future condition; here the condition is childbirth.
شهادة امرأة (shahāda imra'a): Testimony of a single female witness; in Hanafi law, generally not sufficient for hudud or talaq, but acceptable for lineage and maal.
الضرورة تقدر بقدرها (al-ḍarūra tuqaddar bi-qadrihā): Legal maxim: Necessity is measured by its extent; concessions granted by necessity are limited to the specific need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the testimony of a single woman not prove the suspended divorce?
A) Because a single woman’s testimony is never accepted in any case.
B) Because the divorce is not a necessary consequence of childbirth; each can occur independently.
C) Because the husband must also testify.
D) Because childbirth can only be proven by four witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the effect of necessity-based evidence is strictly limited; a single woman's testimony proves childbirth but does not extend to a suspended divorce (per Abu Hanifa, the mufta bihi).

Acknowledged Pregnancy and Suspended Divorce: No Testimony Needed
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether acknowledgment of pregnancy suffices to prove birth for suspended divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether acknowledgment of pregnancy suffices to prove birth for suspended divorce, and apply the trustee (amin) principle to resolve the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Acknowledging pregnancy is like acknowledging birth: the husband's trust in his wife makes her claim sufficient without testimony.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a husband acknowledges his wife's pregnancy and later suspends divorce on childbirth, does her claim of birth suffice? The answer reveals a key difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions, and introduces the powerful legal principle of i'timan (making someone a trustee).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's view: the divorce occurs without any testimony (bila shahada). The wife's mere claim of birth is sufficient.
This is the rukn of the matn.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) require the testimony of a midwife (qabila) to prove birth.
Their view is also within the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The wife is claiming hanth (breach of oath) by the husband, which requires evidence. Childbirth is not automatically proven by pregnancy acknowledgment.
The two companions prioritize the need for proof of the condition.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa argues that acknowledging the cause (pregnancy) implies acknowledging its necessary result (birth). Additionally, the husband's acknowledgment makes his wife a trustee (amin) regarding the timing of birth, so her statement is credible.
The objection of partial separability (infikāk fi al-jumla) is answered by the strong implication and trust.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the wife gets the divorce without proof; under the Sahibayn, she must produce a midwife. This affects the burden of proof in family disputes.
The mufta bihi is often Abu Hanifa's view, but the Sahibayn's view may be considered.

Source Text

Book of Divorce / Chapter on Establishment of Lineage and Custody. And if he acknowledges pregnancy, then suspends [divorce], it occurs without testimony. (And if he acknowledges pregnancy, then suspends) i.e. he suspended her divorce upon her birth, so she said 'I have given birth', and the husband denied her, (it occurs without testimony): this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [companions], the testimony of the midwife is required, because she claims he has broken his oath, so evidence is necessary. [0] And for him [Abu Hanifa]: his acknowledgment of pregnancy is an acknowledgment of what leads to it, i.e. childbirth. And it was objected to him: the matter is not about divorce absolutely, but about divorce suspended on childbirth, and there is no doubt that it is dependent on it, indeed a necessary consequence of it, so it is established by the establishment of its concomitant. And it was answered: that separation in some instances is sufficient to negate complete dependency. [1] His statement: i.e. he suspended her divorce by saying: if you give birth, you are divorced. [2] His statement: 'and he denied her'; i.e. he denied the woman's childbirth and said 'she did not give birth'. [3] His statement: 'without testimony'; i.e. without needing to prove childbirth by the testimony of a midwife or other. [4] His statement: 'because she'; summary: the woman claims against her husband that he has broken his oath by the occurrence of the suspended matter, for breaking an oath in the sense of suspensions means the descent of the consequence, and the husband denies that, so she must provide proof, even if it is the testimony of a woman, for that is enough to establish childbirth. [0] His statement: 'and for him...' etc.; summary: when the husband acknowledged pregnancy, it is as if he acknowledged childbirth, because pregnancy leads to it and is its outcome and fruit, so it is as if he suspended divorce on childbirth while acknowledging it. And there is a consideration: that pregnancy, although it leads to childbirth, is an absolute leading not limited to a time, so acknowledging pregnancy is an acknowledgment of childbirth absolutely at a specific time, but it does not necessarily imply acknowledgment of childbirth at the specific time that the woman claims. And the answer: that his acknowledgment of pregnancy includes making her trustworthy, and the trustee in a claim of returning the trust at any time he claims is believed, so her statement of childbirth is accepted, similar to the statement of returning a trust. (1) i.e. the occurrence of divorce, and the husband denies it, and breaking his oath if he does not fulfill it. See: al-Misbah (p. 153).
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on a second scenario: a husband acknowledges his wife's pregnancy and then suspends divorce on her childbirth. The wife later claims she has given birth, but the husband denies it. According to Abu Hanifa, the divorce occurs without any testimony (bila shahada). According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the wife must produce the testimony of a midwife. The commentary explains that the two companions require proof because the wife is claiming that the husband has broken his oath, and that claim needs evidence. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy implies an acknowledgment of its natural outcome, i.e., childbirth. Therefore, suspending divorce on childbirth after acknowledging pregnancy is like suspending it while already acknowledging the condition. The objection that divorce is a necessary consequence of childbirth and should require proof of the birth is answered by noting that partial separability (infikāk fi al-jumla) is enough to negate complete dependency. The author then discusses a further consideration: pregnancy leads to childbirth absolutely, not at a specific time, so acknowledgment of pregnancy does not necessarily confirm the birth at the exact time the wife claims. The response is that the acknowledgment makes the wife a trustee (amin) regarding the timing, akin to a trustee's statement about returning a trust. This is a critical refinement of the sharh, clarifying the underlying principle.

Key Terms
إقرار بالحبل (iqrār bi-l-ḥabal): Acknowledgment of pregnancy by the husband; it has legal implications for custody and, as discussed, for suspended divorce.
حنث (ḥanath): Breach of an oath or suspended condition; here, the husband is considered to have broken his oath if the condition occurs.
ائتمان (i'timān): Making someone a trustee; here, the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy implies he trusts his wife regarding the birth, so her statement is accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because acknowledgment of pregnancy is a direct acknowledgment of birth at that moment.
B) Because the husband's acknowledgment implies he trusts his wife, so her claim of birth is accepted.
C) Because pregnancy can only be established by four witnesses.
D) Because the two companions require testimony but Abu Hanifa does not.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acknowledgment of pregnancy implies acknowledgment of birth, and the husband's trust in his wife makes her claim of birth sufficient to trigger a suspended divorce without additional testimony.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Testimony in Conditional Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the requirement of testimony in a conditional divorce following acknowledgment of pregnancy, and explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind Abu Hanifa's view.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of pregnancy is acknowledgment of birth — Abu Hanifa's view makes the wife a trustee, eliminating the need for testimony.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a husband acknowledges his wife's pregnancy and then suspends divorce on her giving birth, and she later claims birth while he denies it — does the divorce take effect immediately, or must she provide testimony? The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) turns on whether the prior acknowledgment suffices as proof of birth. By understanding their reasoning (talil), you will see how Hanafi jurists balance textual proof, logical implication, and practical trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
Clearly articulate the facts: husband acknowledges pregnancy, suspends divorce on birth, wife claims birth, husband denies. The dispute is over the evidentiary requirement.
The divorce is mu'allaq (suspended) on birth, not on pregnancy itself.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that no testimony is needed. His proof: acknowledgment of pregnancy implies acknowledgment of birth because birth is the natural and necessary consequence of pregnancy.
This is a qiyas form: the cause (pregnancy) necessarily leads to the effect (birth).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require the testimony of a midwife. Their reasoning: the wife is claiming that the husband has broken his oath (ḥinth), so she must prove the condition (birth) with evidence, just as in any other claim.
The Sahibayn see the wife as a litigant, not a trustee.
4
Analyze the objection against Abu Hanifa
An objection is raised: pregnancy leads to birth in an absolute sense, not at a specific time. Acknowledging pregnancy does not mean acknowledging birth at the precise time the wife claims.
This objection highlights the difference between acknowledging a cause and acknowledging a specific effect.
5
Understand Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa's defenders reply that the husband's acknowledgment makes the wife a trustee (amīn) regarding the birth. Just as a trustee's claim about returning a trust is accepted, so too the wife's statement about birth is accepted.
This shifts the burden of proof: the husband trusted her with the condition, so her word is taken.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy on a conditional divorce. The ruling states that if a husband acknowledges his wife's pregnancy and then suspends divorce upon her giving birth, and she claims she has given birth while the husband denies it, the divorce occurs without any testimony. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) require the testimony of a midwife. Their reasoning is that the wife is claiming that the husband has broken his oath (since the divorce is conditioned on birth), and she must provide proof. Abu Hanifa's argument, presented by the commentator, is that acknowledging pregnancy implies acknowledging its natural outcome — birth. When the husband acknowledged the pregnancy, he effectively acknowledged whatever that leads to, including birth. The commentator addresses an objection: the divorce is specifically conditional upon birth, not pregnancy, and birth is a separate event; acknowledging pregnancy does not automatically mean acknowledging birth at a specific time. The response is that the husband's acknowledgment effectively makes the wife a trustee (amin) regarding the birth, similar to a trustee's claim of returning a trust. Since the husband delegated to her the condition (giving birth), her claim is accepted as long as it is plausible. This dispute highlights the Hanafi principle that an acknowledgment of a cause (sabab) is an acknowledgment of its effect (musabbab) when the cause necessarily leads to the effect. The mufta bihi position in later Hanafi practice is usually Abu Hanifa's view, that no external testimony is required unless the husband explicitly denies the birth.

Key Terms
إقرار بالحبل (iqrār bi al-ḥabl): A husband's acknowledgment that his wife is pregnant.
طلاق معلق (ṭalāq muʿallaq): A divorce that is conditional upon a future event (here, birth).
حنث (ḥinth): Breach of an oath; here, the husband's oath conditioned on birth is broken when the birth occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for a conditional divorce to take effect when the wife claims birth and the husband denies it?
A) Testimony of a midwife
B) Testimony of two male witnesses
C) No testimony is required because the husband's acknowledgment of pregnancy suffices
D) The husband's confession

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes a legitimate ikhtilaf on whether acknowledgment of pregnancy suffices for proof of birth in conditional divorce; the preferred view is that no testimony is required.

Maximum Duration of Pregnancy: Two Years in Hanafi School
Ruling on maximum pregnancy period with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum duration of pregnancy, explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith of Aisha, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-arguments and the Hanafi responses.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school sets the maximum pregnancy at two years, based on the hadith of Aisha and rejection of unverifiable exceptions.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: the maximum duration of pregnancy. The Hanafi school holds it is two years, while the Shafi'i school extends it to four years based on reported historical cases. We will analyze the evidence and the Hanafi responses, understanding how the school defends its position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its primary dalil
Identify the ruling (two years) and the key proof: the hadith of Aisha (ra) reported by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni. Note that such a statement from a Companion in matters not based on opinion is considered as heard from the Prophet.
2
Identify the opposing school's position and their evidence
The Shafi'i school (and others) hold that pregnancy can last up to four years. Their evidence includes the birth of al-Dahhak and Abd al-Aziz after four years, and the story of Umar and Muadh where a child was born after a two-year absence.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each opposing evidence
The Hanafi school dismisses the historical cases as unverifiable (no external witness) and suggests possible uterine closure due to illness. The Umar story is reinterpreted: the absence was close to two years, and lineage was established by the husband's admission, not the pregnancy duration.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The maximum duration affects paternity rulings in cases of long absence. Under Hanafi law, a child born after two years from the husband's absence (or death) is not automatically attributed to him, while under Shafi'i law, a longer period is allowed.
5
Understand why the Hanafi position is mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is relied upon because it is based on a direct prophetic hadith (via Aisha) and rational rebuttal of contrary evidence. The text's tarjih authority favors the Hanafi view.

Source Text

وأكثر مدة الحمل سنتان
قوله : سنتان ؛ الأصلُ في هذا الباب قول عائشة رضي الله عنها : «ما تزيدُ المرأة في الحمل على سنتين، قدر ما يتحوّل ظلّ عمودِ المغزل)، وفي رواية: «لا تزيد المرأة في حملها على سنتين قدر ظلّ الغزل ) ، أخرجه البيهقي والدارقطني. ومن المعلوم أن قول الصحابي فيما لا يعقل بالرأي ، لا سيما المقادير والمدد محمول على السماع عن النبي ، قال السيد الشريف علي الجرجاني في «شرح الفرائض السراجية»: أكثر مدة الحمل سنتان عند أبي حنيفة وأصحابه ، وعند ليث بن سعد ثلاث سنين، وعند الشافعي له أربع سنين ، وعند الزهري له سبع سنين. لنا : حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها ، فإنّها قالت: «لا يبقى الولد في رحم أمه أكثر من سنتين ولو بظل مغزل»، ومثل هذا لا يعرف قياساً بل سماعاً. وللشافعي ه ما روى أن الضحاك ولد لأربع سنين ، وقد نبتت ثناياه وهـو يضحك، فسمّى ضحاكاً، وأنّ عبد العزيز الماجشوني أيضاً ولد لأربع سنين. وروي أن رجلاً غاب عن امرأته سنتين ، ثمّ قدم وهي حامل ، فهم عمر ه أن يرجمها، فقال له معاذ : وإن كان لك سبيلُ عليها فلا سبيل لك على ما في بطنها ، فتركها حتى ولدت ولداً وقد نبت ثناياه ، وشبه أباه ، فقال الرجل : هذا ابني وربّ الكعبة، فأثبت عمر نسبه وقال : لولا معاذ الله له لهلك عمر
والجواب عن الأوّل : إنّ الضحاك وعبد العزيز الله كانا يعرفان ذلك من أنفسهما ولا عرفه غيرهما ؛ إذ لا اطلاع لأحدٍ على ما في الرحم سوى الله الله، ويجوز أن يكون لانسداد فم الرحم لمرض ، فلا اعتداد به.
وعن الثاني : إنّ المراد غيبته عنها قريباً من سنتين ، وإثبات النسب كان بإقرار الزوج.
[1] في «سنن البيهقي الكبير (۷) : ٤٤٣ ) ، و(سنن الدارقطني (۳): ۳۲۲)، و«تهذيب الأسماء» (٣: ١٣٥)، وغيرها.
[2] في السنن الصغرى (٦) : (٢١٤) ، ومعرفة السنن (۱۲) : (٤٦٨) ، وسنن الدارقطني»(۳: ۳۲۲) ، و (سنن البيهقي الكبير»(٧: ٤٤٣)، وغيرها.
And the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years. His statement: 'two years'; the basis of this chapter is the saying of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: 'A woman does not exceed in pregnancy two years, equal to the shift of the shadow of a spindle,' and in a narration: 'A woman does not exceed in her pregnancy two years, the length of the shadow of a spindle' – al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni reported it. It is known that the statement of a Companion in matters not based on opinion, especially measurements and durations, is considered as heard from the Prophet. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif Ali al-Jurjani said in Sharh al-Fara'id al-Sirajiyyah: 'The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years according to Abu Hanifa and his companions, and according to Layth ibn Sa'd it is three years, according to al-Shafi'i it is four years, according to al-Zuhri it is seven years.' Our proof is the hadith of Aisha, who said: 'The child does not remain in his mother's womb more than two years, even by the shadow of a spindle,' and such a thing is not known by analogy but by hearing. Al-Shafi'i's evidence is what is narrated that al-Dahhak was born after four years, his front teeth had grown and he was laughing, so he was named Dahhak, and that Abd al-Aziz al-Majishuni was also born after four years. It is also narrated that a man was absent from his wife for two years, then he came and she was pregnant, so Umar intended to stone her, but Muadh said to him: 'If you have a right over her, you have no right over what is in her womb.' So he left her until she gave birth to a child whose front teeth had grown and he resembled his father, so the man said: 'This is my son, by the Lord of the Kaaba,' so Umar established his lineage and said: 'Were it not for Muadh, Umar would have perished.' The answer to the first is: al-Dahhak and Abd al-Aziz – Allah knows – were aware of that from themselves but no one else knew it, for no one has knowledge of what is in the womb except Allah, and it is possible that there was a closure of the mouth of the womb due to illness, so it is not considered. The answer to the second is: what is meant is his absence from her close to two years, and the establishment of lineage was by the husband's admission.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the maximum duration of pregnancy. The author, commenting on the matn that states 'the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years', provides evidence from a hadith of Aisha (ra) reported by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni, which explicitly limits pregnancy to two years. He then notes that the statement of a Companion in matters not based on opinion (like medical facts) is considered as heard from the Prophet. He cites al-Jurjani's summary of different schools: Hanafi (2 years), Layth ibn Sa'd (3), Shafi'i (4), Zuhri (7). The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Aisha. The Shafi'i argument relies on historical cases of children born after four years (al-Dahhak, Abd al-Aziz) and the story of Umar and Muadh where a child was born after a two-year absence. The Hanafi response dismisses the historical cases as not externally verifiable and explains the Umar story as an absence close to two years, with lineage established by the husband's admission, not by the pregnancy duration. Thus, the Hanafi position is upheld.

Key Terms
أكثر مدة الحمل (akthar muddat al-haml): The maximum period a woman can carry a pregnancy, set at two years in the Hanafi school.
ظل المغزل (zill al-mighzal): The shadow of a spindle, used as a simile for a very short period of time, indicating that even a slight excess over two years is impossible.
قول الصحابي (qawl al-sahabi): The statement of a Companion (Aisha in this case), which is considered authoritative in matters not based on personal opinion, such as durations.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets pregnancy maximum at two years, based on the hadith of Aisha, and dismisses contrary historical reports as unverifiable.

Minimum Pregnancy Duration and Paternity of a Purchased Slave-Wife
Ruling with proof: establishing minimum pregnancy and its application to a slave-wife case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the minimum pregnancy period (six months) and apply it to determine paternity when a man purchases his divorced slave-wife.

Core Idea
Minimum pregnancy is six months; paternity depends on birth timing relative to purchase and type of divorce.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on the minimum duration of pregnancy, which is set at six months based on a Quranic deduction. The passage then applies this ruling to a specific scenario: a man marries a slave woman, divorces her, and later purchases her. The paternity of any child born after purchase hinges on whether the birth occurs within six months of the purchase (indicating conception during the marriage/iddah) or later (indicating conception after purchase). The type of divorce—one or two—further complicates the timeframe for establishing paternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual proof for minimum pregnancy
Locate the Quranic verse (Surah al-Ahqaf 46:15) cited in the passage. Understand how the commentator combines 'thirty months' for gestation and weaning with the two-year weaning period (from other verses) to deduce six months as the minimum pregnancy.
The verse is qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah due to the deduction process.
2
Analyze the base scenario: marriage, divorce, purchase
Study the chain: a man marries a slave woman, consummates the marriage, divorces her, then buys her. The child's paternity is in question. Note the presumption: the child is attributed to the wife (or iddah) if born <6 months after purchase; otherwise, it is attributed to slave ownership.
The marriage must have been consummated for these rules to apply; pre-consummation cases are noted later.
3
Apply the 6-month rule to determine paternity
If the birth occurs less than six months after purchase, the conception is prior to purchase, so the child is from the marriage/iddah and paternity is established without claim. If six months or more, the conception is after purchase, and the child is considered a child of his slave (umm walad).
This assumes the divorce was a single (one) divorce, whether ba'in, khul', or raj'i.
4
Distinguish between one and two divorces
If the divorce was two (the maximum for a slave woman), a severe prohibition (hurmah ghalizah) prevents intercourse after purchase until she marries another. Therefore, paternity cannot be attributed to the owner after purchase; instead, the furthest possible time for conception is before the divorce. Thus, the child is attributed to him if born within two years from the time of divorce.
The waiting period for a slave woman is two menstrual cycles (qur'ayn).
5
Note exceptions: divorce before consummation
If the divorce occurred before consummation, different rules apply. If she gives birth more than six months after divorce, paternity is not established; if less than six months but after a full six months from the marriage contract, it is established.
This clarifies edge cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأقلها ستة أشهر[١]قوله : ستة أشهر ; لقوله في سورة الأحقاف : وَحَمْلُهُ وَفِصَالُهُ ثَلَثُونَ شَهْراً ) كما مر تقديره.
ومن نكح أمة فطلقها فشراها ، فإن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منذ شراها لزمه وإلا فلا ; لأنه إذا كان بين الشراء والولادة أقل من ستة أشهر كان العلوق سابقاً على الشراء ، فهو ولد منكوحته فيلزم بلا دعوى. أما إذا كانت المدة ستة أشهر أو أكثر فالولد ولد مملوكته لأن العلوق أمر حادث [٢]قوله : فهو ولد منكوحته ; لأن قبل الشراء كانت معتدة له أو زوجة له ونسب ولد كل واحد منها يثبت بلا دعوة. [٣]قوله : ولد مملوكته ; هذا إذا كان الطلاق واحداً بائناً أو خلعاً أو رجعياً فإن كان طلقها ثنتين يثبت النسب إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق لأنها حرمت عليه حرمة غليظة إذ طلاق الأمة ثنتان وقرؤها حيضتان على ما مر في موضعه فلا يحل وطئها بالشراء ما لم تنكح زوجاً غيره فلا يقضي بكون العلوق في أقرب الأوقات بل من أبعدها حملاً لأمور المسلمين على الصلاح وأبعد الأزمان هو ما قبل الطلاق فيلزمه الولد إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق وأما إذا كان الطلاق واحداً يحل له وطؤها بعد الشراء بملك (۱) أي بعد الدخول طلقة ; لأنه لو كان قبل الدخول فإن جاءت به لأكثر من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق لا يلزمه وإن كان لأقل منه لزمه إذا ولدته لتمام ستة أشهر أو أكثر من وقت العقد وإن كان لأقل لا يلزمه. وأيضاً تكون واحدة بائنة أو رجعية ; لأنه إذا كان ثنتين يثبت النسب إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق للحرمة الغليظة فلا يضاف العلوق إلا إلى ما قبله ; لأنها لا تحل بالشراء. ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر» (١ : ٤٧٨ - ٤٧٩).
And its minimum is six months. [1] His statement: 'six months'; based on His statement in Surat al-Ahqaf: 'And his gestation and weaning are thirty months' as mentioned earlier. And whoever marries a slave woman, divorces her, then buys her, if she gives birth less than six months from when he bought her, it is incumbent on him (i.e., the child is his), otherwise not; because if between the purchase and birth there is less than six months, the conception was prior to the purchase, so it is the child of his married wife, so it is incumbent without claim. But if the period is six months or more, the child is the child of his slave woman because the conception is a new event. [2] His statement: 'so it is the child of his married wife'; because before the purchase she was either in her iddah from him or his wife, and the lineage of a child in either case is established without a claim. [3] His statement: 'child of his slave woman'; this is if the divorce was a single irrevocable (ba'in) or khul' or revocable (raj'i), but if he divorced her twice, the lineage is established up to two years from the time of divorce, because she became forbidden to him with a severe prohibition, since the divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menstrual cycles as previously mentioned in its place, so intercourse with her is not permissible by purchase until she marries another husband. Thus, it is not judged that the conception occurred at the earliest time, but rather from the furthest time, carrying the affairs of Muslims on the best presumption, and the furthest time is before the divorce; so the child is incumbent on him if she brings it within less than two years from the time of divorce. And if the divorce was one (revocable), he may have intercourse with her after purchase by ownership. (1) Meaning after consummation with one divorce; because if it was before consummation, if she brings it more than six months from the time of divorce, it is not incumbent on him, and if less, it is incumbent if she gives birth after a full six months or more from the time of the marriage contract, and if less, it is not incumbent. Also, it (the divorce) would be one, either ba'in or raj'i; because if it were two, the lineage is established up to two years from the time of divorce due to the severe prohibition, so the conception is only attributed to before it, because she is not permissible by purchase. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:478-479).
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof on the minimum duration of pregnancy, set at six months, and applies it to a specific scenario involving a slave woman. The commentator begins by citing the Quranic verse from Surat al-Ahqaf (46:15) that combines gestation and weaning to thirty months, deducing that the minimum pregnancy is six months after allocating two years for weaning. He then explains the case of a man who marries a slave woman, consummates the marriage, divorces her, then buys her. If she gives birth less than six months after the purchase, the child is attributed to him as the child of his wife (or of his iddah), and paternity is established without need for a claim. If six months or more have passed, the child is considered the child of his slave woman (umm walad) because conception must have occurred after purchase. The commentator elaborates on the type of divorce: if the divorce was one (whether ba'in, khul', or raj'i), the ruling stands as described. However, if the divorce was two (since slave women can only be divorced twice), the prohibition is severe, and the man cannot have intercourse with her even after purchase until she marries another. Consequently, paternity is determined from the furthest possible time, i.e., before the divorce, and the child is attributed to him if born within two years of the divorce. The note clarifies that if the divorce was before consummation, different rules apply. This passage illustrates the Hanafi method of combining textual proof with detailed legal reasoning.

Key Terms
أقل مدة الحمل (aqall muddat al-haml): The minimum period of pregnancy, set at six months by Hanafi school based on Quranic deduction.
أمة (amah): A female slave; in this context, a slave wife.
حرمة غليظة (hurmah ghalizah): Severe prohibition that prevents remarriage between the same couple without an intervening marriage to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The minimum pregnancy is six months; paternity of a purchased slave-wife is determined by whether birth occurs within six months of purchase (for one divorce) or within two years of divorce (for two divorces).
